### Post by ksennin on 2008-05-02
Well, I needed another pc for general family use, so I bought an old system for about US$85, consisting of a Dell GX110, 667MHZ pentiumIII, with 128mb ram, 6GB HD, plus new keyboard and speakers, and a refurbished 17" CRT monitor. The tower was repainted in sleek black and seemed in working order (the looks were important as my wife would have sneered at a beige box).  
The store gave me a year warranty, too!

I added a second 128mb stick I had, plus a 3GB drive to put a second OS in it, and I am planning to dual boot Xubuntu with a trimmed-down WinXP.  The Xubuntu live CD worked sluggish at first logically, but the addition of the second 128mb ram made all the difference and the system ran quite well. Will do full installs this weekend.

A poster asked why waste valuable time in recycling old machines, when the same time invested may have yielded enough dollar value for a newer system. A worthy question, sure, but as I work in civil engineering/structural design, setting up a computer for optimum use is a fun distraction, and a relief from my normal work, one which I may not feel up to doing in the free time.  It gives me personal satisfaction to set things up for my daughter with my own hands as much as possible ( I ran the cables from a lower story to allow web access), and I learn about stuff in the process. 

Also, I think the global culture is finally beginning to learn that short-term cheaper does not always equal better, and as we grasp how rampant consumerism is affecting our environment, making the best of what is around instead of running for the next shiny new thing is actually a moral stance I want to support in all aspects of my life.  I had a second LCD screen, so I sold the CRT for US$30 to a friend at the office who needed to replace his failing one. Buying it independently would have cost him twice that.  So everyone benefited.  And my wife was impressed at how well the "new" system looked and worked and could hardly believe it had set me back only 55 bucks, plus some quite-enjoyed time.

And my daughter loved it.

---

### Post by kagashe on 2008-05-10
Original Specifications:
HP COMPAQ Presario 2500 series Laptop, Intel(R) Celeron(R) CPU 2.80GHz, CDRom, 30 GB HDD, 128 MB RAM, Mandrake Linux 9.1 Tailor made CDs with multimedia codecs and Linuxant internal free modem driver (14.4 Kbps), Radeon IGP 330M/340M/350M video card, Ethernet Card.

Bought in July 2004. Original Mandrake Linux 9.1 worked very well. Then I started using Ubuntu 5.04.

Ubuntu 6.06 Dapper Drake could be installed but was slow, therefore, I added 256 MB RAM to make it 384 MB total. With this RAM it is just suitable for today's Ubuntu. I have started using LXDE to make it faster.

Hardware Changes:
Original HDD replaced by 40 GB HDD, CDRom does not work, LCD screen is broken. I have hooked up external COMPAQ V45 monitor and using it as a Desktop.

Since CDRom does not function I install from ISO.

Difficulty in installing Ubuntu 8.04 Hardy Heron
I have installed from alternate ISO but the new Xorg 7.3 fails to show anything on the external monitor. I can see GDM login screen through broken patches on LCD screen, blindly enter my userid and password, hear the Gnome welcome music and get satisfaction that Hardy is installed.

I can see the console by pressing Ctrl Alt F1, login and try xrandr but could not make X work.

I went to Bugzilla and found a Bug in Xorg for my video card. I have also submitted a bug report.

Till the bug gets resolved using Ubuntu 7.10 with LXDE.

kagashe

Problem solved. I could configure through xorg.conf and it is working with vesa driver. See my post [here]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=4925380&postcount=891").

### Post by linuxlizard on 2008-05-14
I've got two old computer in addition to my main machine that I use.

I've got an old desktop that's 333mhz, 128 mb ram. It runs pcfluxboxos absolutely well, with the exception of youtube videos. However, it's not "slow" and I can run anything I want other than videos and it runs the latest versions well- firefox, gimp, open office, etc.

I've also got an old laptop p3 700 mhz, 192 mb ram that also runs pcfluxboxos and runs everything fast and well- again, I'm talking about the latest versions of software- firefox, gimp, open office, etc. It feels as fast as ubuntu on my amd 2800+ with 756mb, even though it's much less powerful.

I highly recommend pcfluxboxos for older hardware. I've tried them all from puppy to dsl to fluxbuntu and nothing beats pcfluxboxos as far as speed, lightweight power, thousands of the latest versions of apps in the repositories, etc.

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-06-07
It's pretty simple.
Open up a terminal, type sudo cfdisk.
Remove the partition present, and create two (three if you think you'll need swap) new ones.
Then do the HD Install.
The first partition you create _should_ be the bigger one, the second will be the one that stores settings (and the third will be the swap).

---

### Post by ugm6hr on 2008-06-11
@oleksus:
From the Multimedia link below:
> Resolution: To enable the correct display resolution in Ubuntu, you have several options. First of all, there is the fairly useless tool in System > Preferences, the soon to be dead displayconfig-gtk tool, and finally, RandR - the newest method. There will be a graphical frontent (GUI) for RandR soon (keep an eye out for it in System > Administration), but for now you will need to use the command line. Let's say you wanted to change your resolution to 1280x800, you would need to execute the following command:

sudo xrandr -s 1280x800

If that fails, bring up a list of "supported" resolutions with this command:

sudo xrandr -q

Use the first command again and set the highest resolution that RandR claims is supported. Once that is set, try setting the resolution you know is correct, as it may now accept that resolution.

If you're still having issues, press Alt+F2 and type "gksudo displayconfig-gtk" (without "gtk" in Kubuntu), type your password and execute, then select the resolution you want from the list. Some of you may have to select a different screen/monitor in the list before you can successfully change the resolution. Reboot/logout if necessary, then go to Launchpad and report a bug.

### Post by Dr.Ninethousand on 2008-07-12
Recently I got a Thinkpad 390E from ebay..  I set out to buy the oldest Thinkpad I could find, that wasn't so old I couldn't do anything with it..  it also happened to be pretty much the cheapest one in good condition, at $70 (although when it arrived the battery wouldn't hold a charge - I got a new one for about $40)

it came with 96MB RAM, and has a Mobile Pentium II @ 300MHz (actually reported as 298.5MHz)..

with those specs I was able to install Icebuntu with no problems other than waiting a couple of hours, but I found it was just not ready for use, and kind of slow compared to DSL or Puppy..  I also installed Debian Lenny (Beta3 I think) Xfce edition, and found it to be gorgeous and snappy..  I also tried Wolvix which I'm fond of, but it was sluggish..  also, all of these distros besides Puppy had mild to severe issues with power management, with the "acpi=off" boot option sometimes helping somewhat.. I didn't explore other boot options, and the problem may have been easily fixed..  also Puppy was the only distro that was able to get my Asus PCMCIA wifi card online with ease..  if I had a different card or more patience, I may have tried harder with Debian, but even though it ran ok it was a large install for this 5GB hard-drive..

since the RAM for this model is still relatively cheap for it's age, I purchased two 128MB sticks for about $26 and maxed it out, which allows me to load Puppy to RAM for greater speed..

although it's not the most full-featured distro, I really have to recommend Puppy for this laptop, as it works with all hardware out of the box, and with my Asus WL-107 wifi card.. (I just had to use a less obvious alternate wifi app that was in the menu, not the one that comes on the desktop)..  all power management function keys work, USB is recognized, suspend and shutdown work, battery monitor, external monitor, etc..  it's also very visually appealing for such a minimal distro..  boot time is around 2 minutes I would guess..

### Post by boggsm on 2008-07-14
My main computer I use at work is an 600mhz Imac with 384mb of ram.   I run Ubuntu 6.06 on it and I love it.  It is leaps and bounds faster than the pc's running XP.  I work in an environmental lab and we have to submit lab results to the State on floppy disks, which my Imac doesn't have.  These are large csv and text files.  I dug an old 486 w/12mb of ram and a 1gb hardrive out of the garbage and I starting using it for working with these files.  I have Basic Linux installed on it, and it is perfect for what I use it for.  I can use grep, vi, cut etc... and although it has JWM window manager, I use it from the command line.  The more I worked with it the more uses I found for it.  I now have it on the network so I browse the web using links, I telnet to my unix shell account and keep up with the bbs I follow, and I use IRC occasionally.  I had forgotten how productive working with the command line can be.

The other day we had a request to determine the first time we started reporting results for a particular test point.  So this required going through a stack of floppy disks, and searching through this large text file on each one for the first time this point showed up.  One of the tech's was putting the disks in a pc running Windows.  Clicking on my computer, floppy drive, then the file to open it in notepad, then searching for the point using find.  This was taking forever.  I took the disks over to my old clunker, and typed in:
mount /mnt/fd
grep testpoint /mnt/fd/labres.txt
umount /mnt/fd
It took me just a few minutes to search through the whole stack of disks.

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2008-07-14
I went on a dumpster-diving run a couple of days ago to find a replacement for the sort-of old but very crappy laptop that's my MythTV frontend. I picked up three of about 20 old machines sitting out by a dumpster (with permission, of course, always get permission to do that!) and ended up with one working system after I added a couple of parts I had lying around and got a couple of cheap new ones. Here's what it originally had:

CPU: Intel Celeron 900 (Coppermine-128 FC-PGA), 100 MHz FSB
Motherboard: AOpen MX3W Pro-V (Intel i810/ICH1) micro-ATX
RAM: Two dissimilar 128 MB sticks of SDRAM, probably PC100
Video: Integrated i810 video
HDD: WD 15.3 GB PATA/33 
Optical drive: LG 52x CD-ROM (very noisy)
PSU: Hipro 250 W NLX 

I added a passively-cooled EVGA GeForce 256 MB 6200 PCI card as I needed TV out and this card makes a very pretty image on my TV, plus the 6200 does XvMC. I pulled a cheap PCI 10/100 NIC out of my parts box as the unit didn't have a NIC. I also had to get a little $15 Antec socket 370 FC-PGA heatsink for the unit as well. The MX3W originally had a 533 MHz PPGA Mendocino Celeron in it, which made swapping in the 900 MHz Coppermine Celeron a bit of a challenge due to heatsink issues. The PPGA Celerons have a big fat bump on the top of the chip that sort of looks like a thinner IHS on a modern CPU, while the FC-PGA Celerons look like your typical IHS-less laptop CPU or socket A Athlon/Athlon XP with an exposed die on the top of the CPU package. Thus the PPGA and FC-PGA heatsinks are not really interchangeable. The MX3W has literally no clearance around the socket area for a heatsink, so the heatsink that was originally on the Celeron 900 did not fit on the MX3W because it hit four caps and an inductor coil. The Antec unit dropped in perfectly and although it's a bit noisy due to a 4500 rpm fan speed, it cools well and the fan speed is nothing a fan controller can't fix. 

The HDD is a real antique with a <10 MB/sec throughput and makes noises loud enough to make an early WD Raptor sound quiet by comparison. I pulled a 40-pin IDE to 44-pin IDE adapter out of my box of parts and am going to use the far faster, bigger, and quieter 60-gig 2.5" drive from that old laptop in the HTPC instead. This will also let me pull the intake fan out of my desktop's former Cooler Master case that I'm using for this build as the laptop HDD doesn't make enough heat to require direct airflow like many 3.5" units do. Lastly, the LG CD-ROM will be replaced with the Sony CD-RW/DVD-ROM that was in my desktop as its second optical. I ended up not really needing two optical drives in my desktop. Plus the Sony drive will play DVDs and is much quieter than the old LG unit. 

The result is a fairly old computer that will be quiet enough when I am done with it and has plenty of power to be a good standard-definition MythTV frontend (the old Celeron 900 can render the video perfectly at about an 85% CPU usage while just using Xv on the IGP let alone XvMC on the NVIDIA card.) Not bad for about fifty bucks when it's all said and done. It sure beats dropping about $300 to buy new low-end parts when nobody will even be able to tell the difference between the two. And to think that somebody just threw out these parts to get rained on...

---

### Post by Bungo Pony on 2008-07-30

I did some playing with DSL on the weekend and installed it onto a 128M hard drive. However, there were problems that I had to overcome. DSL requires 200M of hard drive space to install. When I was prompted to install the bootloader, I mounted the HD that I was installing DSL on, eliminated the bloat (firefox, xmms, Ted, etc) and then proceeded to install the bootloader, which went without a hitch.

You should definately see if you can upgrade the 64M to something a little larger. I also think that the DSL developers should make a smaller version of DSL without all the included apps since they can be installed from the MyDSL repos anyway.

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-08-08
How much new hardware will you be putting in the Classic!?
You can't seriously expect to run any sort of Ubuntu on an m68k, and by ripping out the CPU you defeat the purpose of having the Classic.
You can still after all get a Linux distro (really old version of Debian) for the m68k that will probably run on the Classic.
As for the G3 Macs, just put DebianPPC on them or something, Ubuntu is too much.

---

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2008-08-08
> **zmjjmz said:**
> How much new hardware will you be putting in the Classic!?
You can't seriously expect to run any sort of Ubuntu on an m68k, and by ripping out the CPU you defeat the purpose of having the Classic.
You can still after all get a Linux distro (really old version of Debian) for the m68k that will probably run on the Classic.
As for the G3 Macs, just put DebianPPC on them or something, Ubuntu is too much.

You can run an up-to-date version of Debian for an m68k machine, although it is not an official build. See here: [http://www.debian.org/ports/m68k/](http://www.debian.org/ports/m68k/) The last version of Debian where m68k was an official build was Debian 3.1 aka Sarge.

If you are more adventurous, you can run other distros such as Gentoo on your m68k. Gentoo would probably result in the greatest number of packages being available to you (as you just get source tarballs and compile, which is largely platform-neutral) at the expense of a very long compile time due to the extremely slow processor in the Mac Classic. m68k is not an officially supported arch, so the installation procedure is a little more in-depth than your average Gentoo install and a whole ton more in-depth than your average Debian or Ubuntu install. However, it's most certainly doable as myself and others have run Gentoo successfully. If you want to look at Gentoo on an m68k, here is a good link: [http://forums.gentoo.org/viewtopic-t-693736-highlight-nano.html?sid=5e05c9147f1919f78657d2f54b145137](http://forums.gentoo.org/viewtopic-t-693736-highlight-nano.html?sid=5e05c9147f1919f78657d2f54b145137)

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-08-29
First of all, if it's old enough to be running Windows 98 and not booting from the CD, you'll want something lighter than Ubuntu, like Zenwalk or Wolvix or Debian.
Secondly, you can use Smart Boot Manager to boot from the CD (assuming you can boot from floppy).

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-08-29

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-08-29
> **Dogalot said:**
> 
I figured something else, but as a newby, not sure which would be a good one to go with (only have used Ubuntu since Jan 1 08)
Smart Boot Manager?  Where can I get that? and how do I use it?

Distro choice depends on the actual specs. You can use thinkwiki.org to find them if you don't know what they are.
There is a copy of Smart Boot Manager on the Ubuntu install disk (in /install)
If you have a floppy drive, get the .img file and use ```
dd if=smartbootmanager.img of=/dev/fd0
```.
(if stands for input file, of stands for output file.)

---

### Post by snowpine on 2008-08-29

> I've got an IBM TinkPad 600 series, at least seven years old. I rescued it from running Windows 2000 (!!) when it had a nervous breakdown. Does anyone know if xubuntu runs those USB RAM drives?

Also, he needs a name. Suggestions? :)

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-08-30
> **matchstich said:**
> i have a thinkpad 701c with 95 on it.  works. need a battery.

but, the ac plugin is ok.


  been trying to figure out to get some form of linux on it. only has a external floppy drive.

8 mb ram, 750mb drive

[http://www.thinkwiki.org/wiki/Installation_instructions_for_the_ThinkPad_701](http://www.thinkwiki.org/wiki/Installation_instructions_for_the_ThinkPad_701)

@rebecca: It is possible to do that with Xubuntu, but -- USB flash drives aren't meant to be used as RAM. XP doesn't do that, Vista does, and what it actually does is use it as a cache for frequently loaded files (like the images for the buttons or the window theme or so on).
Using the flash drive as RAM will end in it's premature death (it will work, but it will be just as bad as swap -- if not worse on something that old.).

---

Also, he needs a name. Suggestions? :)
I usually name my computers after the code name of the processor- I have Coppermine (900 MHz FC-PGA Celeron), Manchester (socket 939 Athlon 64 X2 4200+), and Merom (Core 2 Duo ULV) as my machines. 

Wikipedia tells me that the ThinkPad 600 had either Pentium MMX, PII, or PIII processors, so here are some potential names for your machine:

[LIST]P54C (350 nm Pentium MMX, 120-233 MHz)[/LIST]
[LIST]Tillamook (250 nm Pentium MMX, 200-300 MHz)[/LIST]
[LIST]Tonga (Pentium II Mobile, 233-300 MHz, 512 KB external L2 cache)[/LIST]
[LIST]Dixon (Pentium II Mobile, 300-400 MHz, 256 KB internal L2 cache)[/LIST]
[LIST]Coppermine (Pentium III Mobile, 256 KB L2 cache)[/LIST]
[LIST]Tualatin (Pentium III-M, 512 KB L2 cache)[/LIST]

### Post by brons2 on 2008-09-13
I'm playing with my sister's old laptop currently.  It's a Compaq Presario 700Z.  Specs are 1Ghz Athlon4 (I guess a marketing reaction to the P4), 384MB RAM, 20GB hard drive.  It did have Windows XP on it and has a "Designed for Windows XP" sticker on it.  My sister had previously given it to my mom who used to play around on, but somehow Mom had gotten it to the point where it would get a BSOD on every bootup.

I wiped the hard drive and installed Xubuntu and then the LXDE desktop environment.  For some reason this install does not see the full compliment of RAM, it only sees 233MiB.  I suspect it is because 128MB is soldered on the motherboard and Ubuntu does not have the proprietary Compaq driver to recognize it.  When it had XP it did recognize the whole 384MB.  

It would be nice to have access to that full 384MB as I like to run with several tabs open in Firefox and once you get past about 5 tabs things start slowing down big time as the machine runs out of memory.  Up to that point it works pretty good as you might expect with a 1Ghz proc.  A lighter weight web browser might be another good option.  I was looking into installing Opera but it doesn't seem to be in the Ubuntu repositories.  

Unfortunately, 384MB is max as it takes a PC133 SODIMM of 256 MB max capacity according to the machine specs.  If I could throw a 512 or 1GB SODIMM in there I would.  I don't understand why a laptop manufactured in late 2001 would have a max of 384MB, but it sure does handicap it.  If I could put a gig in here, I think with the proc it has, it would run full Ubuntu quite acceptably.  But these are the constraints I am stuck with, so, I need to run a lightweight type Linux on here to save memory. but still desire as much modern features as possible, since the proc can handle them.

Another problem I need to overcome is the wireless.  At one time, it had a working PCMCIA B\G card in it for XP.  But one day the laptop was hanging out on the floor and someone stepped on the card, hopelessly bending up the PCMCIA pins and metal guides.  So now PCMCIA is no longer an option for any kind of expansion.  

Ergo, I need to find a USB B/G Wifi adapter that works out of the box with Ubuntu.  I looked through some of the hardware compatibility list for Ubuntu and I am pretty confused.  A lot of people report that "the card works out of the box" but then admit in the comments that they had to do X, Y, and Z to get it running.  Considering that this machine is probably going back to my mom (I have a C2D laptop dual booted between Ubuntu and Vista) I need the wireless to work with no muss and no fuss.  I probably have enough skills to get something working with ndiswrapper and/or ndisgtk, but my mom sure doesn't.

I swear, this thing is such a connundrum!

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-09-13
The Damn Small Linux wiki has a list of adapters that work with DSL, and therefore work with Ubuntu.
And Opera can be downloaded from their website in a .deb -- in order to get flash working, you'll need to install flashplugin-nonfree from the repos.

### Post by zmjjmz on 2008-09-28
Of course not.
I have 13 computers, only one of them capable of comfortably running the default configuration of Xubuntu (2006 MacBook).
The second best computer is a Thinkpad T20 with 384MB RAM and a 700MHz Pentium III, and Xubuntu in its default configuration is terrible.
Of course, modifying Xubuntu (replacing gdm with SLiM, removing Compiz, replacing XFCE with LXDE/Fluxbox/IceWM/JWM, turning off a bunch of useless [to me] services, etc.) will make it far lighter, much like modifying XP (TinyXP, nLite) will make it much lighter. But modifying Xubuntu is legal :)
The Thinkpad currently runs Debian Etch with Fluxbox, and it's lightning fast.

---

### Post by snowpine on 2008-09-28
> **The Jinx said:**
> so this doesn't come as surprising to you zmjjmz? I always thought that ubuntu well at least xubuntu would work pretty smoothly on a 800mhz machine with at least 256mbs of ram.

Hi Jinx,

Remember that Ubuntu was first released in 2004. It is designed to run well on a typical post-2003 computer with a capable processor and 512mb of ram. Unlike other flavors of Linux that have been around for 10 years, Ubuntu has no "legacy" of supporting older hardware. 

That being said, I found both Ubuntu 7.10 and Xubuntu 7.10 to be usable on my spare 500mhz Pentium 3 laptop with 256mb of ram. (I had to use the Alternate CD to install.) Both were slow, and I found that they performance boost of Xubuntu over Ubuntu was less than I had hoped (10-20% quicker at most). 

I am currently dual booting Crunchbang (8.04+Openbox) and SliTaz on that same laptop. Crunchbang gave me the performance boost that Xubuntu never gave me. However, compared with SliTaz, performance is night and day (Crunchbang takes 2:45 to boot and uses about 60mb of ram at idle, SliTaz boots in 0:45 and uses just under 30mb). Ubuntu used over 100mb of ram and Xubuntu about 80mb, for comparison.

In conclusion, I will say that Ubuntu and its derivatives are the slowest linux distros I have ever used. However, this sluggishness is more than compensated for, in my opinion, by the incredible user-friendliness, software repositories, and user community. I think Ubuntu is the #1 distro for a reason and would recommend it as my top choice to a Linux newbie with a computer made in the past 5 years (like me, for example).

---

### Post by wilgeman on 2008-10-29
I love reincarnating old machines that were thrown in the garbage!  I used to be a garbage man and one customer threw away a perfectly good laptop, a NEC Versa 486SL 33Mhz 4MB of RAM.  The battery was dead and windows 3.1 would not boot, but I was able to install Basic Linux, muLinux, and FreeDOS on the hard drive by installing it in a external laptop drive enclosure and installing the floppy distros through through qemu.  I could not get XVesa working because the ram is so little, but everything in command line works great!  I also found a HP Pavilion Laptop (850 Duron, 512 MB RAM, and 6x DVD rom) in the trash with a broken hard drive controller, but I use Puppy Linux with a flash drive and live cd to serve as a MP3 and print server.  I also was given a WYSE 50 terminal client with no hard drive 300MHz and 64MB of flash Memory and I can get Puppy Linux to boot nicely on it but hangs when doing web browsing.

There is no such thing as an useless computer, Linux can revive 15 year old hardware to be used for something!

### Post by rbolio on 2008-11-10
Hello! ,  Im doing a project consisting of getting old computers , installing some linux distro on them and then just giving them out to schools with low resources;  I don't have a definite computer model yet i hope most comps will be kinda old....

What would be the best Ubuntu (or linux) distro to use in this project? i was thinking about Ubuntu 7.10 but im not sure where i can get it ^^

Thanks!

---

### Post by snowpine on 2008-11-10
> **rbolio said:**
> Hello! ,  Im doing a project consisting of getting old computers , installing some linux distro on them and then just giving them out to schools with low resources;  I don't have a definite computer model yet i hope most comps will be kinda old....

What would be the best Ubuntu (or linux) distro to use in this project? i was thinking about Ubuntu 7.10 but im not sure where i can get it ^^

Thanks!

Hi there, many of your questions will be answered by reading this thread. I know 62 pages is a lot. :)

Here is another good thread on the topic: [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=575456](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=575456)

It is difficult to make a specific recommendation without knowing the specifics of your hardware. 

I would not recommend Ubuntu 7.10 for one very simple reason: It is not a long-term support release, so its 18 month lifecycle will be ending April 2009. The schools would not be able to get updates after that date, not a good situation. Ubuntu 8.04 is a LTS version and will be supported through April 2011 (as well as its cousin Xubuntu, which is better suited to old hardware). 

That being said, Ubuntu/Xubuntu/Kubuntu is a very modern, resource-intensive Linux distro. It would not be my choice for computers with, for example, less than a Pentium 4 with 512mb of ram (I know that is a sweeping generalization and it's just my opinion). 

Puppy Linux would be my vote for old hardware in a school setting. There are even "puplets" (Puppy remixes) designed especially for educational settings.

Good luck!

---

### Post by snowpine on 2008-12-09
> **jacoody said:**
> I have a PIII 600 with 128MB of ram but I can't get ubuntu or xubuntu to install yet.  It locks up about half way through.

JA

128mb of ram just isn't enough to install by the regular method. You should try a minimal install, then build up from there: 

[http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/minimal](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/minimal)
[https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Installation/LowMemorySystems](https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Installation/LowMemorySystems)

Or try a non-Ubuntu distro. Or get more ram. :)

---

### Post by joninkrakow on 2008-12-09
> **jacoody said:**
> I have a PIII 600 with 128MB of ram but I can't get ubuntu or xubuntu to install yet.  It locks up about half way through.

JA

Have you tried doing a commandline install? Once you get the commanline install up and running, you can then install the X11 environment with something simple like jwm or even icewm. I have run icewm on 128 megs of ram ok. You might also want to try something like PuppyLinux, which runs just fine on this sort of computer. 

-Jon

### Post by ymo on 2008-12-24
My 2nd newest PC cannot even boot the Ubuntu installation CD.

MicroClient JrSX - [http://norhtec.com/products/mcjrsx/index.html](http://norhtec.com/products/mcjrsx/index.html)
366MHz Vortex86sx CPU
128MB RAM
32MB VRAM

The lack of an FPU puts paid to running any flavor of Ubuntu (the 128MB non upgradable RAM does not help). In fact there are virtually no distributions available today that will boot on anything less than a 486DX (without building a custom kernel at the very least).

I've made an IPCop firewall/proxy box with the add-on Copfilter that apart from other services allows me to run ClamAV (which I don't need) & Privoxy (which I do) with no slowdown on surfing speeds.

For anyone interested, here is a link to my escapades with that one:

***_[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=998490](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=998490)_***

Setting up IPCop & my ADSL modem/router is really easy, now that I know how.  It would take me inside of half an hour to install & setup IPCop & set the modem/router into bridge mode.  Though getting there the first time took nearly 6 days of casual work & reliance on a couple of forum users.

I've found some other PII's &  Celerons at the tip, but they have for one reason or another been incompatible with Linux.  I want more of these boxes for 2 puposes, 1. I want a backup of my IPCop box that is setup & ready to go incase the one I'm using fails. 2. I'm hot to build a FreeNAS/torrent slave to store my media on & to download torrents in the background & overnight when required.

The old PII & PIII boxes make fantastic servers for virtually free, apart perhaps for drives that may be required for RAID setups.

I've written quite a rave on these topics over here in the Other OS Talk forum:

***_[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1005192](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1005192)_***

### Post by Kopachris on 2009-01-01

### Post by zmjjmz on 2009-01-01
If you know the model number, it shouldn't be too hard to upgrade the RAM.

Run DSL from a LiveCD, and in a terminal type ```
lspci | grep VGA
```
It should say what kind of graphics card it has so that you can download the appropriate Win98 driver and/or Linux driver.

DSL installation is quite easy. Open cfdisk and format the partition you want as ext3 (ext2 is only necessary if you have a PII or worse), then remember the name of the partition (i.e. hda1, hda2, hda3, etc.). When you do the DSL hdinstall (it's in the menus), just remember to specify the right partition to format.

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2009-01-02
GParted will do the same job as cfdisk. cfdisk is just faster.
I would recommend doing an ubuntu-base install and then installing packages from there, or installing Debian Sid netinstall and doing similar stuff.

DSL is a bit deprecated (based on Debian Woody) so it might not be appropriate for common use. The other suggestions I gave you would only be appropriate once you've installed the whole environment. I would recommend trying a lightweight IceWM setup, much like Icebuntu, but I would _really_ recommend trying to buy some more RAM. If he/she really wants to use it for modern computing needs, you'll need at least 128MB RAM.

Oh, and buy a cheap NIC. [This](http://www.walmart.com/catalog/product.do?product_id=2067722) looks good.

### Post by zmjjmz on 2009-01-03
Install xubuntu-desktop and go from there. It effectively adds the ability to use Xubuntu to the gdm menu (select Xfce in Options->Session), thereby speeding up your system immensely.

### Post by etnlIcarus on 2009-01-11
> **urukrama said:**
> Try a command-line Ubuntu install, and add Xfce (or even Openbox). It will be a lot faster. Xubuntu isn't particularly light.

Have a look [here]("http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntu/minimal") on how to do so.

Honestly, doesn't make much of a difference. If you install Xubuntu, just uninstall gnome-system-tools (which should remove systemtoolsbackends) and you've pretty much reached parity with a command-line install + xfce.

A full Xubuntu install is preferable as you don't have to screw around with things like policykit due to a lack of sane defaults with the command-line install.

Anyway, got debian testing running on an old Pentium MX 200mhz (last of the P5s) and 68mb of RAM. Only thing it couldn't handle was web browsing with kashekase (sp). Tried Xfce 4.4; only problem there was xfdesktop took about 10 minutes to load.

### Post by urukrama on 2009-01-12
> **etnlIcarus said:**
> Honestly, doesn't make much of a difference.

That has not been my experience. Xubuntu (just as a full Ubuntu install) runs quite a few startup processes that are absent in a command-line install. These are perhaps hardly noticeable on more recent hardware, but that make a noticeable difference on older computers.

I wouldn't go for a Xubuntu install (even a modified one) on my computers, but a command-line Ubuntu install with xfce4 is bearable. A minimal Debian install with xfce4 is even faster.

---

### Post by etnlIcarus on 2009-01-12
You *may* be able to save a couple of MB of RAM usage with the command-line install but once you install all the programmes you use, you're likely to have all those background processes running again anyway.

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2009-01-14

Right, which is why Smart Boot Manager is nice. It's a boot floppy that scans all devices for bootable partitions and lists them in a nice menu so that you can boot from them.

### Post by mpsii on 2009-01-14

> I'm not sure that USB technology even existed back then


USB has been around since Pentium II technology and even Pentium MMX. According to Lenovo's website, your laptop should have at least 1 USB. If you have a floppy, the smartbootmanager diskette should boot your USB key.

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2009-01-14
> **urukrama said:**
>  

I'm not sure yet what I'll do with it. Since it doesn't have a CD-ROM drive, I can't boot into an install CD, and I don't have an external floppy drive I can connect to it to force it to boot from an external CD drive. Perhaps I'll take out the hard disk, connect it to another computer, install some lightweight OS on it, and reconnect the hard disk to the laptop. I've seen and heard it done, but have never tried so myself. Does anyone have any experience with that?


You could do that. You will need a USB to 44-pin IDE adapter or a 40-44 pin IDE adapter to connect the laptop HDD to a desktop, though, as laptop IDE is 44 pins and desktop is 40 pins (and the pin spacing is different.) 

You could also boot the machine via Ethernet with PXE from another computer. I've done that to boot a computer with a dead CD drive, no floppy, and USB 1.1 ports that are not bootable.

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2009-01-14
I usually pop the HDD out of my T20 and put the other one in to do stuff like that:
Just use another laptop to do the install.

### Post by cacycleworks on 2009-01-19
> **urukrama said:**
> I just received an IBM Thinkpad 2609, a ...
I'm not sure yet what I'll do with it. Since it doesn't have a CD-ROM drive, I can't boot into an install CD, and I don't have an external floppy drive I can connect to it to force it to boot from an external CD drive.

I had the same problems with a HP e-PC. The dang thing cannot boot from a CD due to something on the mo-board. It didn't have a floppy and something about it didn't want to boot from my usb CD player/burner. I have done the usb boot thing before and it was kinda a pain, so I read up on net-boot.

I set up a net-boot and net-install. I'm a bit above average user, but I just followed the steps to do a PXE? ethernet server on another ubuntu box on my network and had it "point" to an xubuntu install cd copied to the hard drive on that "server". 

It didn't work on the first try, but the ubuntu-docs or wiki for this had some trouble shooting tips, which I followed, and it totally did the install. I would rate it overall as "on the first try" as it all went down in one sitting.

Also, look for a boot without a CD thread on here somewhere.

:) Chris

### Post by OneMixDJ on 2009-01-28
BigBig5,

I think you might have better luck with DeLi Linux as opposed to DSL. Besides, DSL (if memory serves me right) is 50MB of disk space whereas you only have 3MB per the specs you provided.

According to some research I did, the highest memory upgrade you can get for your Fujitsu C340 is 96MB; if you can find it. I know that PNY has a 64MB upgrade (part number P-FPCEM07) for your Lifebook that you can shop around for. Toss in a 20GB HD that you can get cheap off of eBay (I only paid $20 for mine with shipping), you would have enough to install and run Ubuntu.

Hope this helps...

Regards,

OneMixDJ

### Post by mpsii on 2009-02-25

Try Xubuntu or a command line install of Ubuntu with vanilla XFCE4.4. Or, try e16 or e17 or LXDE. 

Your biggest problem is using Ubuntu (default of Gnome 2.4.x) which LOVES RAM... just like KDE. The main issue is not so much your system as the Desktop Environment being used eating away at your ram.

### Post by ranch hand on 2009-03-17
After reading a bunch of this thread I realize that this box is pretty new.

We were given an "old" HP pavilion that didn't work.  It had 128ram and a cooked 5G HDD.  I had a 10G HDD laying around and bought 2 256Mb DIMM ram cards (these are cheap), as that is the max the MB will run.

Running Ubuntu 8.10.  Ran DSL to diagnose the 5G HDD and installed it on the 10G HDD untill the ram cards came.

---

### Post by zmjjmz on 2009-03-31
> **racepres said:**
> 
BTW How would one go about cloning a lappy hdd onto a desktop hdd? I want to have the same "stuff" [Ubuntu 8.04 Hardy Heron]that is on my older dell lappy onto an even older but operational desktop.
Use dd
From a LiveCD
hda1 = laptops harddrive
sda1 = external drive
Replace example values with appropriate values.
```
 dd if=/dev/hda1 of=/media/sda1/lappy.img
```
hdb1 = desktop's hard drive
```
 dd if=/media/sda1/lappy.img of=/dev/hdb1
```

---

### Post by cacycleworks on 2009-04-04
Here's a whole thread about it! 
[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=427540&goto=newpost](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=427540&goto=newpost)

Also if you look back through my posts, I talk about it. I was in the same situation with a pentium 4 hp e/pc. I set up another ubuntu box to be a tftp server with the install image on it. Once the hp e/pc box picked up the image upon startup and began the install, I stopped the tftp server.

Good luck!
Chris

128MB RAM
333Mhz
3,7GB HD
Trident Graphics card
Ubuntu Feisty Server running Fluxbox (i had 3 WM installed at once, last week ;) )

This is supposed to be a place where we share applications/solutions meant to be light and responsive on an old computer. This is one of the many reasons why I love Linux: your computer doesn't have to be top notch to run a fully up to date system.

You can also share Window Managers, Distributions, anything you want :)


Right now I'm using Galeon/Seamonkey as the Web Browser, and they are really responsive. Galeon spends a little more RAM, but it renders pages much faster, and it's very responsive, and it uses my GTK theme, so that's a bonus.

As a file manager I chose PCManFM, and I just love it. It's very very responsive, and the interface is very intuitive, it's a bit like Thunar.

MSN Client: Pidgin. It's hard to find light graphical MSN clients. Now or then I use centericq though, but I actually don't need to. Since i trim down in everything else, at least I can spend something in my messenger, and Pidgin is not a hog at all.

Media Player: every once in a while someone will send me videos in msn, and I use xine to watch them (xine-ui package), and I use XMMS for my music, but I don't use them much.

Right now, conky marks 57% RAM used with a web browser, a file manager, and a messenger client running, and my system is not laggy at all!


Cheers

Got a Dell Optiplex GX1 yesterday (PII, 192mb ram).

Had to reset the bios *(removed cmos battery)* because of some strange bios limitation *(it locks you out of the bios if you open the case)*.

Runs [Slitaz]("http://www.slitaz.org/en/") now, I will be using it as a home server (ssh, mediatomb upnp server, music server).

It has a 6gb hdd and I added an old 30gb hdd and it's hooked up to my 500gb external hdd.

It comes with the emelfm2 file manager, JWM windows manager (or you can use E17)some sort of dock, gparted, alsaplayer, firefox, ...

I was surprised how much they got into that 30mb iso.

I run it headless, but the fact that it has a GUI is great should things go wrong.

--

I would have preferred to use the [Amahi Home Server ]("http://www.amahi.org/")(great project based on Fedora), but it's a crappy pc.

### Post by Bungo Pony on 2009-05-16
> **EnglishSparrow said:**
> I almost forgot, I have a commodore vic 20 and commodore 64 in my  basement. ;)


21k of ram!


Actually, I believe the Vic20 has 5K of RAM. Unfortunately, BASIC takes up a huge chunk of that, so the user is left with 3.5K of useable RAM. 

You can get 21K if you jam a 16K RAM pack into the back.

---

### Post by Bungo Pony on 2009-05-16
> **mikeypizano said:**
> Its actually pretty useless. I have no way to get data on or off of it.

I looked that thing up on Wikipedia and found this...

*An RS-232 compatible serial port was provided*

If you connect that up to the serial port on your PC, you should be able to transfer data back and forth with it. You can install Minicom in Linux, and your little 95LX already has telecommunications software built into it. You should be able to transfer text files back and forth with it.

I don't know how much storage that little thing has, but you could potentially upload some DOS games to it.

And if you don't want it, I could use another toy ;)

### Post by aristotlewilde on 2009-05-16
I got an email alert telling me there was new activity in this thread and I got a little nostalgic.

Some of the best times I've had w/ Ubuntu is running it on older equipment.  Although at times (the 900mhz Celeron desktop I inherited for example) it was maddening, I always enjoyed it.  W/ a little TLC, a highly outdated video card thrown in, and Enlightenment alternated w/ Openbox, that machine served me well and was a lot of fun!

Now I am running lean and mean on much newer equipment, and everything USUALLY works correctly and fast.  It's just not as fun to tinker, and I don't pop over to Openbox enough to mess around anymore.

Suddenly that empty PC case sitting in the attic is looking like a fun little blank canvas...

### Post by Thingymebob on 2009-05-19
Just did a crappy old compaq PII 466 for family. built for NT4 with ISApnp ATI Rage Turbo video, max of 256MB of sdram (pc100) installed, but refused to recognise any disk over 10G, Installed ubuntu-minimal (8.10) dist-upgrade to 9.04 then LXDE.  Spent some time playing with sysv-rc then profiled boot. and it runs reasonably well. Used a greasemonkey script with firefox to force all flash to low quality and I'm pretty pleased with the web browsing experience and overall performance even runs Ooo fine.

### Post by Skippy_X on 2009-05-20
Cool Thread.

I work in a refubishing shop for a nonprofit charitable organization. We recycle or refurbish old systems. Most of the stuff for sale in the shop is P4 class, 1.8-3.0 GHz. We break down most of the P3 machines and harvest whatever components we can for sale to people looking for parts. Most of what we get is recycled - it's way too old to be useful to most people.

It's a bit of a geek playground.

I've got three of those old P3 IPaqs (exactly like the one of the front page, only 700-800 MHz, 512 megs 133MHz ram, 40 GB) on the shelf right now. I loaded Xubuntu on them and priced them at $80 (including keyboard, crt monitor & mouse). I figured they'd be great web browsing machines for a spare room - the type of thing someone could toss into a kid's room and not have to worry about them borking the install w/ malware.

We haven't sold one yet. As I said, it's an experiment. I was just curious to see if there was a market for that sort of thing.

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2009-05-27
> **medic563 said:**
> My problem is that I still have most of my old computers. My wife hates that. I have a 386, 486, IBM x86, Celeron 600, Pentium II 450, (2) Omnibook 4150 with P-II 350 & 400, Celeron dual proc 1000, etc. Even a Commador 64. Don't have my Timex Sinclair anymore though. Just looking for an OS to put on some of these paper weights.

Realistically, it is pretty difficult to use a machine for any real purpose that cannot handle at least 128 MB RAM, does not have PCI slots, and has a CPU slower than about a 100-200 MHz Pentium or equivalent. That's pretty much the slowest machine that can be turned into a file, media, DNS, Web, or print server, which is pretty much the least-demanding task a computer can do. I would say that any machine that can't handle at least 384 MB RAM and a ~700 MHz Athlon or PIII or better would be outclassed as a "basic use" word processing/Web browsing appliance or a low-definition MythTV machine. So if your wife made you choose what machines to keep, keep the C64 since it has value as an antique, the Celeron 600 if you want a server, and the dual 1 GHz Celeron since it can be used as a general-use machine. The rest would only be useful for very specific tasks or just for fiddling and would be my choice to give the boot to if my wife was needing me to get rid of parts. 

Luckily, my wife hasn't asked me to get rid of any parts as I have most of them in active use or packed neatly away as spares in a small box in the closet. Otherwise, I have a few old machines as well:

- Athlon XP 3200+ w/1 GB RAM: MythTV machine, my wife *loves* that :popcorn: So it's not really all that old, but it was still a given-away machine I managed to snag.
- Pentium III 1.0B w/256 MB RAM: my file server, rescued from a trash pile at a local computer store.
- Core 2 Duo U7500 w/1.5 GB RAM: not old at all, but this is part of a tablet with a broken and irreplaceable center hinge. This is usable as a desktop machine if you plug a monitor into it and it is not currently being used, but might end up being my next HTPC after I get an HDTV that has an RGB input. It is little and stored nicely in a little box in a drawer, so it is unobtrusive :p
- Celeron 900 CPU + motherboard: spare for the file server (it was sitting in the surplus pile and I wasn't sure if the PIII unit actually worked.)

### Post by Z_o-s-o on 2009-06-04
The oldest PC I have running Ubuntu is actually one of my first.

Its a Gateway Essential 500, built 2/10/00, and it came out of the box with:

-Pentium III 500mhz (Katmai)
-64mb Ram
-10 gig 5400 rpm hard drive
-8mb Nvidia Vanta gfx card
-Cdrom drive
-Sound Blaster 128D
-56k modem

After all these years I still have it and heres what its like now:

-Pentium III 750mhz (Coppermine)
-768mb Ram
-120 gig Maxtor HDD
-128mb Radeon 9200
-Cdrw/dvd combo drive
-SB Audigy soundcard
-3com Nic

Now THATS how to push an old system and keep it running a modern OS. Runs compiz pretty smooth, but it'll get choppy if you start pushing the Cpu, but thats why compizicon comes in handy. Scores a 441-450 in Geekbench with Compiz turned off.

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2009-06-11
> **mister_p_1998 said:**
> Will it overclock?
Steve

It should as AMD made Thunderbirds run up to 1.4 GHz. However, you need to have some pretty good cooling on that T-bird to get it to overclock as the T-birds tend to run pretty warm as-is. If your chip is a Socket A unit, you're much more in luck as there are some decent Socket A heatsinks out there, but not so much for Slot A.

---

Open that box up bud, see what's in it.  If it's a Socket 428, then you're in luck.  I actually have a socket 428 Thunderbird sitting in front of me right now.   It's one of those "kickin`" overclocking cores.  It's a 1333mhz version!

Anyways, the Thunderbirds/Duron processors used the Socket 428 pattern, so if you have one, I'll send you this chip for free.   I used it in my old system and worked when I pulled it. This thread has "inspired" me to fix my old system.  

AMD Athlon XP 1700+ 
I put in three HDD's today, 40GB WD, 20GB Maxtor, and a 13.2GB WD
GeForce 4 Ti-4400
SB Live! X-Gamer
1GB PC2100 DDR
All running on my MSI KT266a Pro2u mobo I bought back in 2000!

That was the last time I really played with Ubuntu until I finally got a copy of 9.04!   Now 9.04/Vista Ultimate x64 on this machine.  However, no sound thanks to the lame X-Fi card in it...    But it works great.

Anyways, I remember all the troubles I had with that system and getting 7.10 installed properly. I had to fight to get the GF4 to work with compiz, the sound wouldn't get to 5.1 at all, and the worst.. a USR modem.

I fought with that modem for two weeks!!!!   It was nearly impossible to get working, but once I did, no more Windows on that one!   I ran Ubuntu 7.10 for a good year and a half before I dropped that machine for this rig..   3.0 C2d E8400 with 4gb of ram LOL  Big difference.

But, today, I slapped enough parts in it to get it running again.   I also have a cheap old AGP style Matrox card here if you want it mister_p.   Just let me know.   :)

I also have a couple 144-pin?  SDRam chips of unknown sizes here as well.   And the best part, I found my old P3 450mhz...  the top end V1 Katmia core!   Let's go with that again LOL!     This chip, which is hanging on my wall in memory of my best % overclock ever on a P3...   She hit 700mhz and wasn't the least bit unstable!     The Intel 440bx chipset died and I never looked back when I built my Athlon system.

As a matter of fact, in memories sake..  I was looking through my spare parts bin and found a 128mb Dimm of Rhambus...  RDRam...   LOL      Keep it up guys, and thank you for inspiring me to boot up my nearly 10 year old XP 1700+ rig again!

### Post by AoSteve on 2009-06-13
Ok, I figure I better explain some of the process as it's been tooo fun already.  

First, I use a satellite provider for internet service, so I can't just freely download the newest version of Ubuntu.   So, I'll get the 32-bit version tonight.   I didn't have a 32-bit copy of anything linux aside from 7.10.  

First attempt at boot, it got to the CD-Prompt and just shut off.   ...   Hmm..  Time to break out the tester..   Yep, the 12v on my power supply drops to 0 once it's powered on.  Looks like a dead PSU to me.

So I tear into some old PC's I had laying around and found a 200w PSU from a Gateway P2 350Mhz system that has been sitting in the back room with a dead mobo forever and a day.   I hook it up, boom it fires RIGHT up.   Amazing..

Well, the install took forever and a day seeing as how it has a WD400 5400RPM 40gb HDD in it.  Once installed, I had nothing but horrid trouble with my router/lan connection.   After some nitpicking with it, boom.  I'm internet bound.   

It's wild though, that old machine pushes out 1366x720 to my TV through a DVD-D->HDMI cable.   I'm gonna be toying with it for some time to come.   Here's the specs right now, and it's slow, but loaded down right now.

XP 1700+  @ 1100mhz because the CPU fan is dead and I need to get a new one.  (got an external fan on it to keep it cool enough for now)
1GB PC2100 DDR 266mhz ram!  Woohoo!
40gb WD drive, the other two are dead as doornails :\
Some offbrand CD-Rom  LOL!

I'm going to try and install 9.04 tommorow, hopefully it works out well LOL!

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2009-06-13
> **AoSteve said:**
> 
Open that box up bud, see what's in it.  If it's a Socket 428, then you're in luck.  I actually have a socket 428 Thunderbird sitting in front of me right now.   It's one of those "kickin`" overclocking cores.  It's a 1333mhz version!

Anyways, the Thunderbirds/Duron processors used the Socket 428 pattern, so if you have one, I'll send you this chip for free.   I used it in my old system and worked when I pulled it.   I think the cooler is around here somewhere, stock but worked decent.   It's an AYHJA core and I was able to push it to 1.9Ghz with a nice custom air setup.  I also have a 256dimm of PC2100 DDR1 here as well.  If you want them, just pay the shipping and I'll send them to you.  :)

Athlon Thunderbirds were either Slot A (SECC 232) or Socket A (PGA462.) There is no socket 428- did you inadvertently mash together the two first Intel Pentium 4 sockets, which were Socket 423 and Socket 478?

[quote[This thread has "inspired" me to fix my old system.  

AMD Athlon XP 1700+ 
I put in three HDD's today, 40GB WD, 20GB Maxtor, and a 13.2GB WD
GeForce 4 Ti-4400
SB Live! X-Gamer
1GB PC2100 DDR
All running on my MSI KT266a Pro2u mobo I bought back in 2000!

When I replaced it, I took my HDD and formatted it for this sytem's "picture" storage drive.  It's a WD800JBSE!  One of the first drives with the 8mb cache!  Ultra ATA 133!! Woohooo!  LOL

That was the last time I really played with Ubuntu until I finally got a copy of 9.04!   Now 9.04/Vista Ultimate x64 on this machine.  However, no sound thanks to the lame X-Fi card in it...    But it works great.

Anyways, I remember all the troubles I had with that system and getting 7.10 installed properly.   The only things that worked after the install was the basics like mouse/keyboard and the like and my sound.   I had to fight to get the GF4 to work with compiz, the sound wouldn't get to 5.1 at all, and the worst.. a USR modem.

I fought with that modem for two weeks!!!!   It was nearly impossible to get working, but once I did, no more Windows on that one!   I ran Ubuntu 7.10 for a good year and a half before I dropped that machine for this rig..   3.0 C2d E8400 with 4gb of ram LOL  Big difference.

But, today, I slapped enough parts in it to get it running again.   I also have a cheap old AGP style Matrox card here if you want it mister_p.   Just let me know.   :)[/quote]

Such a system is still very serviceable, particularly if you're running Linux. I use a slightly faster Athlon XP (3200+ Barton) with 1 GB PC3200 and it runs my standard-definition MythTV setup absolutely flawlessly, almost to the point of being overkill.

> I also have a couple 144-pin?  SDRam chips of unknown sizes here as well.

SDRAM chips were 168-pin if they were desktop parts. The sizes go up to 512 MB for unregistered modules and 1 GB for registered server RAM. A lot of the SDRAM I've come across is 64 MB and 128 MB stuff, with some 256 MB sticks every so often. The 512 MB modules are rather uncommon to come across in old machines as people generally had to go out and specifically buy SDRAM modules that big.


> And the best part, I found my old P3 450mhz...  the top end V1 Katmia core!  Let's go with that again LOL!     This chip, which is hanging on my wall in memory of my best % overclock ever on a P3...   She hit 700mhz and wasn't the least bit unstable!     The Intel 440bx chipset died and I never looked back when I built my Athlon system.

It's the "Katmai" core and the 450 MHz unit you have is actually the slowest of the desktop PIIIs. The top-end Katmai was 600 MHz. However, if you got it up to 700 MHz, you probably got one of the highly-sought-after SL35D batch that had the 300 MHz SRAM cache chips from the 600 MHz unit instead of the normal 225 MHz SRAM cache chips that were supposed to be in the PIII 450. The SL35D units routinely could reach 600 MHz if not a bit more, like yours did. Ah, the good old days, where it took a really special chip to overclock more than 15% or so... :D These recent Core 2s, Core i7s, and Phenom IIs make overclocking by a huge margin so easy a caveman could do it!

> As a matter of fact, in memories sake..  I was looking through my spare parts bin and found a 128mb Dimm of Rhambus...  RDRam...   LOL      Keep it up guys, and thank you for inspiring me to boot up my nearly 10 year old XP 1700+ rig again!

Maybe you did have an old Socket 423 P4 Willamette or one of the horrid i820 PIII motherboards kicking around somewhere if you have RDRAM. I don't envy you there- working with godawful-expensive RDRAM and its "continuity RIMMs" and stuff was no fun, particularly when the Athlons using cheap SDRAM or DDR wiped the floor with the P4 Willy and PIII.

### Post by AoSteve on 2009-06-15
On the back of the socket it should say what it is.  It'll be something like "Socket 462" or I've even seen some say "Socket A"  If it's a KT266a chipset, it should be able to run Athlon XP chips up to I believe 2800+.  :)   (I should reiterate...    It'll say on the part of the socket that is raised compared to the PGA layout. :))

It could be a Slot A processor, but those pretty much ended with the AMD K7 line after the 1Ghz boundry was broken back in the day.   The Thunderbird and Duron line was the first to use the 462 setup, so if it's a 462, I got a processor for ya.  :)

Now sure on how much the shipping would be, I'm sure I could get ahold of UPS or USPS and find out though.   :)   I actually have two working AGP cards now LOL

### Post by AoSteve on 2009-06-20
If you go to radio shack you can get a switch that is powered by 2-5v   I used a 5v, since the led's are 5v.   Then hotwire the power to the switch and then to the lighting LED's.   It takes some electronics knowledge, but doable.   I got the powered switch, can't remember the name.  I had to make a custom feed from a 4-pin molex then ran it directly into the powered switch.   The switch only activates when it sees the power from the led light from the mobo then allowing the molex's power to the added LED's.   Simple, but I'm not sure if you can find those switches easily.  I'll look around later when I have time and find the one I had.  :)

Nice laptop too!  LOL  Very.. custom heh

### Post by The Real Dave on 2009-07-14
One of my older computers :D Its actually the guts of a Packard Bell computer, but the case of that was so fugly it deserved death :lolflag: This Emerald case is nicer lol :D It used be my main server, but has been replaced by a much more powerful and quieter PIV. The trouble with this computer is that the fans that cool the CPU and the graphics chip are very small 50mm or less, and make a hell of a lot of noise. 

So I wanted to make it quieter, so it could be a backup/webserver, and not have to turn it off at night so I can sleep. 

The larger the fan, the lower the noise, but vibration is a problem too. However, no such thing as vibration when your fan is suspended in midair :D

In the second pic you can see the other small fan, the noisest of the lot :confused: Thats going to be gone as soon as I make a proper chipset heatsink and a shield to tunnel some of the air from the fan down at it. CPU can survive on passive cooling.

451Mhz Pentium III
128Mb PC-100 SDRAM
6Gb 5400rpm IDE
8Mb Voodoo Graphics Chip

### Post by tomasrey88 on 2009-07-29
mister_p_1998, that's a quality computer that you have there. The power supply of your computer used enormous EM coils to transform AC to usable DC. The bigger the coils, the sturdier the power supply. This is the best way to do it. Sadly, to cut costs, most modern power supplies for computers use very lightweight EM coils. This makes the power supply very flimsy. Often, computers fail due to a failed power supply. The coils overheat and melt. This causes AC electricity to flood the motherboard and the computer fries. That computer you've showed me will last FOREVER. 

Early VCR recorders made in Japan or the USA had real METAL gears inside them and lasted FOREVER. New ones made in China use nylon gears (not even plastic!). It's just disposable crap, now. 

Early computers were built tough, the same way. My Apple (my first computer) never crashed, never broke, and worked fine up until the day it was retired. 

I think, in order to get a quality computer nowadays, you'll have to build your own. I'm gathering parts to build a quad core super computer with a huge heat sink and instead of a loud, lousy cooling fan, I've got some old Sharper Image air purifiers that I'm going to use as SILENT cooling fans. Cool, eh? I'm also going to build a switchboard for it. You know how you have to turn on and off certain peripherals? Most people just leave them on, but I HATE wasting electricity. Instead, I'm going to make a switchboard for my computer where I can flip a switch on the front of the computer to turn a peripheral on/off. Instead of a drab metal box, I'm going to make the case out of wood. Now, THAT's a computer! If possible, I'm going to buy disk drives that run on metal gears for durability. Also, it will run off of an enormous solid state flash drive array instead of a hard drive for durability....

---

You want to be careful with wooden cases Tomas, look great, but no RF shielding! I looked into this many years ago, and the best idea seemed to be a steel case with a wooden outer veneer. I had this idea about a PC inside an old-fashioned writing desk, cooling was a problem too.

### Post by ksennin on 2009-08-09
> **hans12345 said:**
> I guess I found the answer elsewhere in these forums:

  Re: recommended system requirements for xubundu,ubuntu,kubuntu
  The system requirements for K/X/Ubuntu are really low.
  They can be found here:    [https://help.ubuntu.com/community/In...emRequirements](https://help.ubuntu.com/community/In...emRequirements)
For any official ubuntu derivative 128mb ram is too low to install, unless you go with the base/alternate and add to it. Not for the beginner.

The system requirements listed on the page are very optimistic. I would not recommend xubuntu (the lightest official ubuntu version) for anything below 800mhz/384mb ram. My recommended minimums would be 1.0ghz/512mb.

Crunchbang, u-lite, fluxbuntu would be derivatives more suitable, or other distros altogether.

---

### Post by blueturtl on 2009-08-13
> Am I better off installing a base Debian (or other) system and installing Openbox + a FM?

Having myself very recently used Debian on a P233 with 64 megs of RAM, I say go with Debian base.

Spin-offs like Damn Small are great for getting to know what apps might run well on old systems, but I find Debian to be more manageable and the repositories and support are a little better.

Haven't used OpenBox myself, but I understand Fluxbox might be a little faster.

Here's what I used when I had that rig:

Email: Mutt or Claws-Mail
IRC: irssi
Jabber/MSN/etc: Centerim-UTF8, or PSI for a GUI app
Web browser: Opera/Links2 (Links2 will display graphics under X)
Bittorrent: rtorrent
Music player: moc
Video player: MPlayer
File manager: Midnight Commander "mc"
Terminal Emulator: rxvt-unicode

I had no problem running X.org, and it was in fact quite speedy in the running, but graphical apps would quickly bog the system down due to limited RAM. That's why even under X I would mostly run console applications.

---

### Post by texasjim on 2009-08-14
> **sideaway said:**
> Can anyone help? My compaq won't recognise any live CD's bar Ubuntu 7.10 (and up), Puppy Linux and Damn Small Linux, I'm really keen to get SliTaz going. But it won't recognise it as bootable media...

Can anyone help?



I thought I had the same problem yesterday installing on a PII system my brother gave me. The whole shootin match, including the cd-rom drive is from the late 90's I suppose.  Anyhow, the cd-rom would only read from 700MB CD-R media. I have memorex and maxell CD-RW and it would not read the re-recordable media at any speed. I created it on 2 separate drives on 2 separate machines, but no go. Finally used a Sony CD-R written at 2x speed and it worked like a champ.  So, if your cd-rom drive is old, it may be the disks themselves it doesn't like.

[http://old-releases.ubuntu.com](http://old-releases.ubuntu.com)

Older versions of Ubuntu are better on older hardware, newer versions are generally heavier on resources and are optimised for newer hardware... Whereas if you get an Ubuntu version from the hardware's era...

### Post by DFlame on 2009-08-31
[Compaq Armada M300](http://h18000.www1.hp.com/products/quickspecs/10381_div/10381_div.HTML). Praise the lord, it has **a** USB port!

I put a cli install of Jaunty on it via unetbootin, as it does not have a CD/floppy drive and doesn't boot from the USB port. Building from that, it now runs xfce4 using epiphany as a browser.

Getting it on the internet was surprisingly simple too. It supported an Edimax (rt61 chipset I think) PCMCIA wireless card out of the box, and it can alternately use a bluetooth dongle to hitch on my cellphone's connection.

It currently serves as a lightweight travel computer. I'd rather be walking about with a £30 computer than a £600 one ;)

### Post by blueturtl on 2009-09-21
> **johnny2m said:**
> What is the oldest hardware that people have got flash video working on (smoothly), and with what distro?

My experiences with Flash:

AMD K6-III 400 MHz with Debian: Flash 10 is a no go on that one. Trying to play any video will be a slide show.

PowerPC G3 500 MHz (an iMac): ok job with OS X, but it was borderline unwatchable.

PentiumIII 1 GHz with Debian: Flash 10 works but it is sensitive; if you try to open multiple videos or browse in another tab while playing a video you get skipping.

Flash is very power hungry.

### Post by SydneyGB on 2009-09-21
I've been reading through some of the posts in here and I'm amazed at what people are saying will run on an old computer!  

I have a PII 364mhz machine (old IBM Thinkpad 360E) with approximately 11GB of HD space (don't ask me.. that's what Gparted says!) and 128 mb RAM.  Currently it has Win98 on a 2GB partition -- I just discovered the other unallocated 9GB!  That's what you get with a hand-me-down computer I guess. :)  Since the Windows is beginning to act up (and I would love to have a portable Linux machine I can show off!) I thought I'd try some Linux on it.  DSL runs in RAM flawlessly, though I have a certain inability to run some packages on it.  Puppy is okay.  I would love to have a heavier distro on it, but my Wubi install of Xubuntu 8.10 here on this desktop machine leaves me leery about anything that heavy.  I'm considering Crunchbang, which I'm getting used to in VirtualBox.

Someone said they were running Ubuntu 8 or 9 on a very similar machine.  Could this seriously work?  Don't get my hopes up, now people... give it to me straight.  When I installed Xubuntu on the desktop (256MB RAM, 5GB for Ubuntu) I was advised the machine is not fast/modern enough to run that.  But the laptop is even older.  Am I missing something?

I'm eagerly learning all I can about Linux through books, internet research, and good old trial-and-error.  I have .isos for probably a dozen distributions and I've tested most of them out in VirtualBox.  I'd love to turn this little old laptop into a dedicated Linux box.  :)

For ease of discussion, the laptop I should like to have (X)ubuntu on is called Parnaparnti and has the following stats:
IBM Thinkpad 390E laptop
128M RAM
11 GB HD (possible bad sectors causing that odd number)
PII 364mHz processor

The desktop is called Kurdnatta:
Dell Optiplex GX1P desktop
256 MB RAM
10 GB HD (5 available - sharing with Win98 for scanner usage, etc)
PII 398 Mhz processor

Now that I look at their stats side-by-side, as it were, maybe they're not so different after all, save for the memory.  Xubuntu runs okay on Kurdnatta, so possibly a regular install, with all 11GB for Ubuntu on Parnaparnti would run more smoothly?  What do you folks think?

### Post by Siljrath on 2009-10-10
i got an old laptop second hand (maybe third or fourth actually), for free.


16mb ram.
133mhz pentium processor.
1gb hd.
seriously failing power supply and a cable connection that barely stays on.
0x usb slots

i have deli linux 0.7 on it at the moment, with ion added as an alternative wm, just to help oot the speed a little bit, ken. ;)

if i was better able to find parts and to know how to find out from my machine or somewhere which parts i need, then i would really love to kit her out with bigger hd (so i can multi-boot some light distros) and fix the power-supply and battery... oh, and add usb!   can that be done to old laptops like that?


i remember how swift and capable and responsive my desktop was back in good ol 96.  and then 98 too when i got a much more powerful machine and encumbered it with win98 instead of 95.   so if win 95 could be *that* cool (in terms of being massively less bloat laden than subsequent windozes, AND faster than many heavy distro set-ups with gnome and kde ousted specifically), then why the massive demands on our resources these days?  hundreds of mb!?  it's not enabling me to get a feel like i'm doing that many more times stuff, i'm not getting that much of a multiplication of boon from all the stuff it's doing...   it's wasted, it's bloated.   i like that i have my 133mhz with 16mb ram.  that will enable me to see if i'm getting my punch's worth outta my software, so i can maximise whati get outta my hardware, leaving more power for me, less power for mr torvalds' monster and it's minions.
i shall love to also get my 266mhz laptop revived... its "dead" unexplainedly.
both of these would be great as an intentionally bottlenecked testbed for any distros i intend to use, so that it make's sure it's tiiiiight, with no wastage.   that way, when i put it on my modern 2gb ram machines, i'll know that my ram is filling up from what i'm doing, not what the os is doing.  ....   i know there are holes in that theory, but that's just the generalisation i'm using to help give myself yet another reason for adoring these relics, and keeping them alive so that one day i can show them to my great great grand kids and say "see what we used when i was a teenager?

haha!  i would have to get win95 installed in my dream multi-booting ancient laptop too.


i wonder if that thing would even know what to do with a 4gig hd, let alone anything over 160.... and ssd!?   think i could find enough savvy out there to know how to stick a ssd in that old hitachi laptop?   surely there's some conversion drivebay thingys for that no?    cos hd speed was the bottleneck even back then.    imagine how tight a competitor you'd have to modern machines if u could just get a couple more o those bottlenecks pushed outta your old machines.


also, now, i know these aren't new, but those tiny pcs, like from [http://www.aleutia.com](http://www.aleutia.com) , they are so sweeet!!  :D way of the future?   seems almost like a return to where we were a few years back and the direction desktop's should have gone.
i wonder how long it will be until someone ceates a sandwich modular micro pc, just add slices of what ya fancy.  from 7mm to as thick as you like.  hehe.   just dreamin, haha, sry.

so what's out there that's faster than deli?

tinycore?   :D


yeah, i might stick around n read more of this thread later. :D :popcorn:

### Post by texasjim on 2009-11-05
I would use the alternate install cd for Ubuntu rather than the livecd install. You have more control over which packages are installed, and the alternate install cd works in 384mb. Then use Remastersys to build install cd's for your remote systems and transmit or ship them there.

  Good Luck
  Jim

### Post by cacycleworks on 2010-01-20
> **chumkila said:**
> my next step is to investigate what I can do to take the standard install down to as minimal possible installation. I only require this system for surfing and light media capabilities.

Yaaay! Cool. From the alt CD, you can install a server version. Then use sudo aptitude to preview installing various packages to see how much bloat there is. 

We have a server at work that normally has no monitor. But I installed xfce via aptitude which enables the X environment to run. Then I picked and chose which apps to have. This way if I needed a "desktop" to do something to the machine via a GUI, it was there. I normally command line everything, but there are times when I know there's a visual tool that's more efficient. 

Aptitude is great in that you use / to search for packages then "+" to add them. Hit "g" once and you are presented with a screen showing all dependencies and packages it wants to install. Hit "g" again and it starts downloading and installing. Aptitude is a little confusing at first but is TOTALLY worth the time and effort to learn, as it truly lets you see what's happening behind the scenes. 

For a learning exercise, go in aptitude and "+" add something you DON'T have like kubuntu-desktop. Then hit "g" once ... and you can page down through all the numerous packages that go along with a kubuntu install. (be sure to quit before hitting "g" again) 

From a server (command line) install, you can add any of the desktop metapackages to see what they want to install by default. While in the server command line environment, I recall you can have multiple terminals by Alt-Fkey combinations...

:)

### Post by ranch hand on 2010-01-20
Get the server edition of the Ubuntu of your choice.  This will be text only so you have to use apt-get or aptitude to get the rest of what you want.

Install the desktop you want.  The Gnome desktop environment isn't too bad either.

The main 9.10 download page has a minimal CD that you can get to that is tiny.  It is a net install type thing.  Only about a 12Mb down load if I remember right.  I got one and upgraded it to 10.04-testing and then put the Gnome desktop on it.

EDIT
This may interest you too;

[http://www.lifehacker.com.au/2009/05/install-a-minimal-ubuntu-desktop/](http://www.lifehacker.com.au/2009/05/install-a-minimal-ubuntu-desktop/)

### Post by ranch hand on 2010-03-25
Can't get it big enough to read.

However, if you are on old hardware I would say that is a pretty good boot speed.  The 10 second goal is for booting on some mini computer running on an atom cpu with a SDD.  You can look up what the specific box they are aiming the boot speed at.

There is no way older hardware is going to get that kind of speed.

My box is in my sig and the best I am doing so far is about 36 seconds.  Haven't checked lately as bootchart does not work on most of my installs anyway with any reliability.

Some are almost 2 minutes.  They are all on the same hardware.  This is not something for real concern at this point in development.

If there is not better performance across similar hardware after release I think that plymouths days are numbered.

I do not suggest removing plymouth but I have a number of installs and occasionally remove it from one.  Speed really goes up then.

### Post by Tom.Gee on 2010-03-25
> **mr-woof said:**
> I'm noticing a lot of people are using their older machines for media machines etc, what software do you use for that? It is easy to setup? 

Does anyone use older machines for storing backups etc?
Do you mean Media Machines as in players? Or, as I've seen, older machines as a concentrator - a place to keep all your mp3s, jpgs, and mp4s stored, as well as any data you may wish to back up, so that there aren't, for example, some on my machine, some on my wife's, some on my son's, etc...

I keep a machine for just that.  It is a PIII with a pair of four port SATA PCI cards and a stack of 700GB to 1.5TB hard drives.  I use an Argosy media player, (This one is similar: [http://www.geeks.com/details.asp?invtid=HV335T](http://www.geeks.com/details.asp?invtid=HV335T)) and I samba into //Khaless, (named for Khaless the Unforgettable) to get to the common media stash.  //Khaless keeps backup copies of everyone's documents and data.  Understand that I'm new to Ubuntu, but have been backing up data to a remote since the first time I had a major data loss back in 1980.  I did this on my TRS-80 systems. (Does anyone remember the command Prot :n RUF - [Reset Update Flags] that you would do after backing up all the updated files?) Anyway, in the MSDos days batch commands like this would make quick work of backing up changed data:
```
NET USE L: \\KHALESS\jimmyshare /PERSISTENT:NO
xcopy D:\*.* L:\ /M /S /E /C /I /F /H /R /K /Y

```
Such a batch file would copy any file updated on your data drive (D:\ in the above example) to your network share, then reset the archive bit so that next time, only files newly modified would be copied.  Batch files of this type still work under Windows.  At some point I will learn how to do the same thing in Linux.

As for what software, the above mentioned are simply built in features of the various operating systems.  I've read here that to share an Ubuntu volume with Windows PCs, it is just a matter of enabling Samba and creating your shares.  Check this old Samba tips thread: [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=202605](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=202605) or maybe this more advanced thread: [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1330637](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1330637)

I await your response with interest.

Regards

HH
:confused:

### Post by ranch hand on 2010-04-18
Memory for that box would be cheap and you can probably find it new in the original anti-static packaging on ebay.

I would look the MB up and find what the max is for it and get that.  I am sure that is in the range that would run just about anything with more ram.

I have Ubuntu 8.10 running on an HP Pavilion xt993 very nicely, if somewhat slow.  It only had 128MB on board when we got it but I got a pair of 256Mb cards for $10.00 including shipping.  I ran DSL on it until the new ram came.

I do not think your better half would be amused by DSL.

You might try a minimal install (net install).  You get a download of about 12MB that when burned to a disk will boot you up and connect you to the repos with a cli so that you can install the DE of your choice.  I would try Lxde.  It might run on the ram you have and it is lighter than Xfce.
