### Post by Objekt on 2010-04-21
I am restoring an ancient Compaq Presario 710.  Bought it in 2002 for around $1000.

The sticker on it says "Designed for Windows XP," and indeed it shipped with Windows XP Home, but this is a pack of lies.  256 MB RAM is by no means adequate for Windows XP, especially when 16 or 32 MB RAM is consumed by the built-in video device (S3 Twister-K). 

It always ran XP slowly, but it doesn't do much better with Xubuntu.

I'd like to install a super-lightweight distro on it, like Puppy Linux.  Problem is, the optical drive is shot.  I had to install Xubuntu over the network, since the machine is also too old to support booting from a USB device.  There is no "alternate install CD" for Puppy Linux, as there is for Ubuntu.  Rumor  has it, there are ways to trick Puppy into installing over the network, but I have not done it yet.

I have a lead on a cheap, replacement optical drive, so I hold out hope that network installs will no longer be necessary.

The Presario is surprisingly heavy, compared to recent laptops.  While the screen is not especially large (14.1"), the computer weighs over 6 pounds.  Current, comparably-sized laptops (e.g. Dell Latitude E6410) weigh about 1/3 less.

### Post by klemes on 2010-04-24
I have a closet of old computers :
I will tell you only for those which are currently at running order starting from the older:

- A Dell Optiplex GX1 PII350MHZ 192MB RAM ???HDD ???GFXCard Desktop
It was given to my ex-wife at a sorry state as a used computer with only 64MB RAM and XP preinstalled on it.She used it mainly for word processing (teacher)and boy the sound that the HDD made continuously swapping was incredible .You could swear that  the disk would explode at any moment now.After a couple of years I got her an Intel Core2Duo repalcement so I got my hands on it  and I just fell in love with the way it was assembled.....I cleaned it up ,I upgraded the memory and of course installed Linux on it rightaway,initially aiming for Ubuntu but due to low RAM the LiveCD thing was not suitable for it so I turned to Debian(in 4.0 Etch version back then)which was happy to run all day (I even got to it to play flash videos satisfactorily believe it or not) and overall it was a very pleasant useable little machine for day to day usage.Still font of it

-AMD Duron 1000MHz 256MBRAM 40GBHDD NVIDIA GeForce2 MX400 SiSKS75A M/B Homemade Tower

-IBM NetVista P41.8GHz 512MBRAM 40GBHDD 16MB NVIDIA RivaTNT Desktop
An overall well known honest little good-looking machine with proven processor adequate RAM this time the only culrpit being it's rather weak videocard(even for it's age)My particular machine used to be owned by a bank (meaning 24/7 being turned on not a ver clever buy ha...?)that upgraded their computers and selling their previous line of equipment to resellers and that's where I got mine.The reseller being a close friend of mine and initially reluctant to sell it to me but I was captivated by it's black-bluish stealth looks and I never looked back again so the rest is history.
Did a lot of work in it too.First I have upgraded the memory with an other 256 DDR module so as to double the initial memory capacity.Then I upgraded the CD-ROM drive with a CD/DVD-RW combo.And the more extreme  thing I did in this computer which boosted video performence to acceptable levels was to overclock the NVIDIA card and flash it with a TNT2/MACH64 BIOS after lots of experimentation resulting in a highly creditable for such an old card 360FPS in glxgears OPEN-GL test!!!!!:)
It currently runs Debian 5.0 Lenny with Enlightment 17 as graphical environment and it is a highly useable machine overall!!:guitar:

It's amazing to have a full-featured GUI in only 256 MB RAM, on a whopping 20 GB hard drive.

I also tried Puppy Linux 3.41.  I installed Crunchbang because, being based on Ubuntu, it's much more familiar.  Also lots more software is available for Ubuntu-like distros.

I'm getting hold of another old laptop, and my intention is to use it for distro experimentation. I've been using Linux [Ubuntu] for a few years now but I feel I'm not getting further with it, my hope is that by playing around with the lighter, simpler distros, I will able to learn quicker the ins and outs of GNU Linux. I'm finding it overwhelming to familiarise myself with the shear number of programs that embody a typical Ubuntu installation.
There are a lot of great distros out there.

One thing you might want to try, though is Ubuntu netboot install.  Small download, around 12MB.  You get a kernel and a terminal and that is about it.  You can take it anywhere you want from there.

All that stuff that is installed with a normal install is due to "meta" packages.  Ubuntu desktop is the main one.  Gnome desktop is pretty much the same thing without anywhere near the bloat and there are other desktop environments.

I like that type of install as you can have it as small as you want while still having the entire Ubuntu repo built in and at your finger tips via apt.

### Post by eckeroo on 2010-05-22
Right,

I've replaced the 6GB hard disk with a 40GB hard disk [IDE, 40GB 5400rpm Hitachi HTS541640J9TAT00] and have decided to give Arch Linux a try. The step-by-step beginners guide provides a brief explanation of each of the bits you install and configure. I now have a better understanding of things like X, daemons, openbox, etc..

The graphics card was tricky. Satellite Pro 4280 laptops come with a S3 Savage IX/MV graphics card with 8MB onboard ram - not enough for 24 bit 3D acceleration. These use the xf86-video-savage driver. To enable 3D acceleration, you need to reduce the colour depth to 16 bit but the X auto configuration defaults to 24 bit.

To get round this I created a Xorg.conf using the command 'Xorg -configure' and then 'cp /root/xorg.conf.new /etc/X11/xorg.conf'

After hours of google searches and repeated attempts to modify the Xorg.conf to set the colour depth to 16, I finally stumbled upon the required modification:

[PHP]Section "Screen"
             Identifier "Screen0"
             Device     "Card0"
             Monitor    "Monitor0"
             DefaultColorDepth 16
             SubSection "Display"
     ****rest of Xorg.conf******[/PHP]

Adding **DefaultColorDepth 16** at that point in the Xorg.conf worked. Ran X and no errors occured in the /var/log/Xorg.0.log and the DRI [3D acceleration] was enabled.

Those xorg.conf files are a nightmare: I don't understand how X can have varying xorg.conf layouts/instructions/commands between graphics cards. It was by trial and error I got this to work.

I've yet to actually test out the 3D acceleration as I'm still installing programs to my Arch Linux. I'll probably try out a screen saver that uses openGL.

:)

---

### Post by jdos2 on 2010-08-08
I have an old Thinkpad a20m (700 MHz Pentium-III/512MB RAM) that I occasionally waste my time trying to make "useful." I have not yet found a Linux distribution that I can get working completely.
Debian/Ubuntu don't have the MACH64 DRI stuff. It was only fixed four years ago, so I understand the normally conservative Debian folks don't want to even include it as an option anywhere. Their patches rip the module out.
So that rules out Debian/Ubuntu if one wants DRI. Compiz won't run because there's just not enough video memory (8MB) but applications like VLC can make use of the DRI presentation space... 
Well, one has to load Fedora to do THAT.
Fedora has support for the Mach64 in the kernel, but it suffers from what ALL modern Linuxes seem to suffer- a broken CS46XX sound driver. Now, Debian doesn't even include that driver in Lenny (the Debian folks didn't like the blob in the kernel driver, so requested permission from the CS folks- several years ago), though the BackPorts kernel does, and it WORKS! No stuttering while adjusting the volume! No stuttering in general! Give Puleseaudio a decent priority, and the laptop actually becomes nice to listen to- it's got pretty big speakers and a nice sound.

This all applies to building kernels too. Build a Debian patched kernel, no Mach64. Ubuntu is the same. Fedora- the audio driver is STILL borked, even with the most modern version of Alsa, if one figures out how to get all that working together. 
It boils down to a choice of whether I want to watch or listen to a movie- vlc will play fine with the mach64 DRI and even at full screen won't lose frames, but the audio is so bad that it's impossible to watch with any pleasure. Only one version of Lenny, with the BPO kernel, works well enough for audio, but it's only a few frames a second.

Debian Lenny (stock) installs the ondemand governor that WORKS with the Pentium-III, but does so "incorrectly" (as per Debian bug report) and is "fixed-to-broken" on anything but mainstream Lenny. The secret is to install a userspace governor, but THAT took a good while to track down. Battery life went from two hours to an honest four.

I kinda wish the laptop didn't have such a nice keyboard. It's the stick against which I measure all others. Sitting in the closet right now, it's quietly waiting for me to pick it back up again and have another go. Maybe someone'll look at the unassigned bug reports and fix stuff. Maybe not. It's old hardware. 

XP works fine on it, though.

---

:)

### Post by blueturtl on 2010-09-03
> **Skriblinmatt said:**
> What are the Ispci & Isusb? Here's what I found on the card: Linksys Wireless-G Notebook Adapter Model No WPC54G ver 3.1; MAC 00226B9947C6 Serial No. BDH81HA03447; FCC ID Q87-WPC54GV3; IC ID 3839A-WPC54GV3; here's the # next to the bar code 6102-81607011R. On the front it says 2.4GHz (802.11g). Hope that helps. Where are you going to look and what are you going to look for?
 
(P.S. Not too scared, after all, the challenge and the community are the reason I'm playing this game ;D)

A quick Google on your card turns up the following information:

It's based on some form of Broadcomm chipset (the worst case scenario).
There is a driver that works with some Broadcomm chipsets, but it requires you to acquire propriatory firmware files from the Windows driver package (the 'fwcutter' method). Another solution is to actually use the Windows driver with Linux (the 'ndiswrapper' method). Personally, I suggest you trade that thing for something compatible, especially if it's a pcmcia card (the type you can just slide into the laptop from the side). It will save you a lot of headache and should only cost about $10-15.

'lspci' and 'lsusb' are commands that you can enter into a terminal emulator such as Gnome Terminal (Applications->Accessories->Terminal). 'lspci' lists all detected PCI devices, and 'lsusb' all the USB ones. It allows us to easily find out what your computer ate without having to take the whole thing apart. :)

Let us know which course of action sounds preferable to you. :)

### Post by MugOTea on 2010-10-05
I've got loads of this technical-junk... IBM Intellistations, and very old work stations, mixed up with slightly more up to date equipment... Only problem is, it's my setup and not just an experiment! lol

Since I discovered Ubuntu, every machine I get my mits on gets treated to Mugs n mugs of Ubuntu...

That said, my latest conquest over Windoze is giving me a headache!

My young nephew saw me playing games on Ubuntu and was instantly hooked. He then told me his laptop had packed in because of a virus. Being an IT engineer/all rounder I told him to drop it off and I'd stick Ubuntu on it - jobs a good un, bye bye virus...

NOPE!
The Fujitsu Silent Laptop HDD was just a little bit too quiet... and an absolute bugger to find. It's a TINY A360+ Notebook and after removing all the panels, and the keyboard I still couldn't identify the HDD caddy... Turns out to be squished under the front panel, to the left of the touch pad, on-top of the FDD.

Transplant a donor HDD and install Ubuntu and job done, I thought, but no again... The graphics work fine for basic X and Internet browsing and I thought it was ok for this laptop, shame about some of the games though... Then I discovered that the integrated graphics card actually has a bit of memory "ProSavageDDR" and should allow some 3D functions... so I do some investigating and find out the video card is reported as "Unclaimed" by Ubuntu... The S3 ProSavage drivers are loading, and not reporting any errors (just success) in the Xorg.0.log...

Googled it search here, there - found it its an open and unattended bug... bummer...

Some one suggested I implement the openchrome drivers instead, I tried that and did something wrong, I created a Xorg.conf file and it only contained one device that I typed in manually (can't remember it all now but was along the lines of:
<device>
  Identifier="{product name from lspci}"
  Driver="openchrome"
<enddevice>

Turns out I've bricked the laptop and have to start all over again. As soon as the laptop started it threw a "Low graphics mode" error, none of the options it gave me worked except from viewing logs - which informed me it was my stupid config file (please bear in mind it was late)... But I couldn't even get a terminal login... I even tried using the Ubuntu disc live session to delete the /etc/Xorg.conf on the hard disc... which obviously failed...

I'm still no wiser with my graphics card problem, but the OS is nearly reinstalled... It's always the 'quick jobs' though, isn't it?

I can do all of the basic stuff, I can even play media from my ipod! (My music is stuck in my desktop) Of course that doesn't make much sense, since I can use my headphones...Anyway, the only thing this baby can't do well is watching videos (Youtube videos get choppy) but I even got to enable some transparency for show off ;) it's only for the screenshots though...

Here are my specs:

8 year old Toshiba Satellite 4060CDT
128MB RAM
333Mhz
3,7GB HD
Trident Graphics card
Ubuntu Feisty Server running Fluxbox (i had 3 WM installed at once, last week ;) )

This is supposed to be a place where we share applications/solutions meant to be light and responsive on an old computer. This is one of the many reasons why I love Linux: your computer doesn't have to be top notch to run a fully up to date system.

You can also share Window Managers, Distributions, anything you want :)


Right now I'm using Galeon/Seamonkey as the Web Browser, and they are really responsive. Galeon spends a little more RAM, but it renders pages much faster, and it's very responsive, and it uses my GTK theme, so that's a bonus.

As a file manager I chose PCManFM, and I just love it. It's very very responsive, and the interface is very intuitive, it's a bit like Thunar.

MSN Client: Pidgin. It's hard to find light graphical MSN clients. Now or then I use centericq though, but I actually don't need to. Since i trim down in everything else, at least I can spend something in my messenger, and Pidgin is not a hog at all.

Media Player: every once in a while someone will send me videos in msn, and I use xine to watch them (xine-ui package), and I use XMMS for my music, but I don't use them much.

Right now, conky marks 57% RAM used with a web browser, a file manager, and a messenger client running, and my system is not laggy at all!


Cheers

### Post by jhsu802701 on 2010-10-10
Use antiX Linux.  It works great on this computer, a 2001-vintage IBM Netvista desktop with a 1.0 GHz processor and 256 MB of RAM.  It also works great on my older computer, a 2000-vintage Dell desktop with a 467 MHz processor and 384 MB of RAM.

antiX Linux is lightweight, has a superior repository (Debian compatible), and is user-friendly.  Other distros can match or even beat antiX Linux on one or two of these criteria, but none can on all three.

### Post by amjjawad on 2010-11-11
Laptop

[LIST]
[*]HP Omnibook 4150
[*]CPU: Pentium II @ 366MHz - 256KB Cashe
[*]RAM: PC-100 64MB
[*]HDD: 4.5GB
[*]Wireless Adapter: Cable & Wireless NC10CW3
[*]USB 1.0 Port
[*]No LAN port
[*]No Floppy
[*]No CD-Drive (dead)
[*]Windows XP
[/LIST]
This laptop belongs to one of my relatives who is not using it and leaving it to dust so I thought it would be good idea if I could install Linux and use it for some basic stuff.

Until now, I guess I tried more than 20 Distributions and couldn't install one of them. Spent many days trying that but nothing happened.

- BIOS doesn't have any option to boot from USB so had to install Plop. I'm able to boot from USB now.
- During installation (that in case I could reach that point), the installer program was searching for the CD-Drive and couldn't go further with that because it's dead so installation aborted.

I have a [thread]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1590614"), I know it's marked solved but actually it's not. I marked it solved because I thought I found what I was looking for.

I guess the only way to deal with this problem is either to connect the HDD to another PC and install from there or use an external CD-Drive. I don't want to buy any extra hardware as it's not mine after all and I'm doing a favor to my relative and that laptop to install Linux instead of a very slow Windows XP (however, it's up and running) not to mention it's old (+11 years) and I don't think it's good idea to spend anything for it.

The whole point was just for fun and experience. I love sometimes to deal with old stuff.

### Post by urukrama on 2010-11-11
> **zaivala said:**
> GigaG, have you tried Mint or Peppermint?  Both are Ubuntu-based, the main difference being the Peppermint leaves all your apps on the cloud rather than loading them to your computer. 

I've seen this suggestion a few times for old computers, but most people forget that older computers don't handle the applications in web browsers very well--those are actually fairly resource hungry!

With such low memory, I suggest you go for a command line system. [K.Mandla's blog]("http://kmandla.wordpress.com/") has some good suggestions (though you might have to dig a little).

### Post by amjjawad on 2010-12-17
> **kreigmonch said:**
> Thanks I have also been looking at DSL and Puppy. Do you happen to know what sets all these Distros apart? All the choices are kinda bogging me down.

[http://distrowatch.com/](http://distrowatch.com/)

Use the search and choose "old machines" and you'll get a list of Distributions.

I have installed Mint 9 GNOME on an antique laptop with only 64MB and 366 PII. I know it was dead slow but the laptop didn't blow up ;)

DSL and Puppy are not my cup of tea. I prefer many other options.
If antiX or Slitaz will work fine with you, stick with these two :)
Otherwise, you might find other alternatives.

Try to use google and you'll find many useful stuff.

### Post by Spice Weasel on 2011-01-09
Close unneeded processes/programs, clean out the case if it is dusty, disable any compositor you use (use a lightweight desktop if possible), keep the room cool and if all else fails get an external fan.

I have a Dell Inspiron 5100 laptop.

Specs:
15 inch display (~31 cm)
60 GB HDD (upgraded from 30 GB)
1 GB RAM (upgraded from 256 MB)
2.4 GHz single core Intel Pentium 4 processor
ATI Mobility Radeon 750 video with 32 MB RAM

I don't think it qualifies as crappy; however, it is from about the 2003 time-frame.

I have Ubuntu 10.04 running on it. I had to be conservative with enabling visual effects. It still gets between 2 and 2.5 hours battery life. It only got 3 hours battery life when it was new. ### Post by Hur Dur on 2011-01-09
I'm looking for a distro that will run in 128mb RAM or less with a GUI. Not that I NEED it, I have 512mb RAM, but I would like to have a light one. Right now I am using a custom Debian net install with Fluxbox, but it still uses about 100mb RAM idle with Conky.

I've tried:

SliTaz - I like it a lot, but I can't see myself using it as a primary desktop. It's more of a rescue CD.

Debian - Main desktop right now. It's nice, but takes up more memory than I would like.

Tiny Core - I like it. But, just like SliTaz, it seems to be more geared towards being a rescue CD than a desktop distro.

KolibriOS - Again, I like it, and it runs smoothly in 16mb RAM, but it is not a Linux distro, and it barely has any packages other than default.

Puppy Linux - I just dislike this one in general. How it became so popular is beyond me.

DSL - I love it, but it is extremely outdated.

Ubuntu - Another distro I just dislike in general.

---

### Post by Spice Weasel on 2011-01-09
Have you tried AntiX, SimplyMEPIS 8 Minimal or Wolvix?

Might also be worth checking out Alpine Linux or Arch. They are command line distros, but definitely worth looking in to.

> **Hur Dur said:**
> Tiny Core - I like it. But, just like SliTaz, **it seems to be more geared towards being a rescue CD than a desktop distro.**

That makes about as much sense as a milk flavoured hammock. TinyCore is meant to be the successor to DSL.

---

### Post by Hur Dur on 2011-01-09
Thanks for the suggestions, I will try them out. I have used Arch in the past, but it seems that my graphics card hates it. Every time I start X in Arch, I get my desktop copied like 6 times, but each of them are so blurry that I can't even use them.

Has anybody tried NetBSD? It has a reputation of running on anything and everything. It has even ran on a toaster. Also, I tried out Archbang, it uses about 70mb RAM on startup. It's a lot of RAM for ancient hardware, but I could see having a functional desktop with 128mb RAM using it. Of course, you can always install a window manager like JWM for boosted performance, but it comes default with Openbox.

### Post by Hur Dur on 2011-01-11
I tried two distributions today. Arch Linux, and ConnochaetOS.

I recommend them both for older hardware. A new install of Arch with Openbox uses about 30mb of RAM idle. It didn't surpass 100mb of RAM usage when I was browsing the web with arora, and doing some stuff with the terminal.

ConnochaetOS used about the same amount of RAM idle, but it was a complete package. It came with a lot of packages installed. The only thing that did not work was sound, but that can be fixed using alsaconf. It didn't go past 57mb of RAM usage when I was browsing the web. I was even able to listen to some music using Mplayer at the same time. It is pretty much Arch Linux for Pentiums, but it is pre-configured and easy to use.

---

### Post by mister_p_1998 on 2011-01-14
> **Hur Dur said:**
> I'm looking for a distro that will run in 128mb RAM or less with a GUI. Not that I NEED it, I have 512mb RAM, but I would like to have a light one. Right now I am using a custom Debian net install with Fluxbox, but it still uses about 100mb RAM idle with Conky.

I've tried:

SliTaz - I like it a lot, but I can't see myself using it as a primary desktop. It's more of a rescue CD.

Debian - Main desktop right now. It's nice, but takes up more memory than I would like.

Tiny Core - I like it. But, just like SliTaz, it seems to be more geared towards being a rescue CD than a desktop distro.

KolibriOS - Again, I like it, and it runs smoothly in 16mb RAM, but it is not a Linux distro, and it barely has any packages other than default.

Puppy Linux - I just dislike this one in general. How it became so popular is beyond me.

DSL - I love it, but it is extremely outdated.

Ubuntu - Another distro I just dislike in general.

Have you tried Vector Linux? its very good on old hardware and fast too.
Steve

### Post by ThatBum on 2011-01-14
I have two. One is a machine from 1988, and the other from 1996. The first is a no-name 286 box with 640k of RAM, a 70MB hard disk, and VGA graphics in 256 colors. The 286 runs at a blistering twelve megahertz. It doesn't have any sound aside from the PC speaker. I have no idea about its origins, it's older than I am, and I never asked about where it came from. It has Windows 3.11a / DOS 4 on it. It has a 5.25" floppy drive. In addition to its serial mouse and keyboard, it has two MIDI joysticks (!) and a dot maxtix printer that is obnoxiously loud. It's cheap too, the damn pins kept breaking off the pin carriage and made all the letters it prints a mottled, illegible mess. No fancy power management here, just a fracking huge red toggle switch on the side of the case hardwired to the power supply. Ahh, good times.

The other machine is a Pentium I 166MHz with MMX (and that's it!), 32MB of RAM, and a 4GB hard disk, which was huge at the time. It has true color VGA, integrated sound, a 3.5" floppy drive, and a 4x CD reader. It was custom assembled for my grandpa to use for work at the school administration. I opened it, and all the parts have little handwritten notes taped to them about batch numbers and things, and the invoice was taped to the inside of the side panel. Weird. Also there was literally a spider's nest jammed in the HDD cage. It came with Windows 95, but that install died, so 98 got put on it, but THAT died, so it sat unused for about a decade after grandpa got his PIII machine (which he still uses). I recently dug it out and recovered the important data from it, and put Damn Small Linux on it (stripped-down Knoppix), and it hasn't had a problem since. It has APM. It also has a Turbo button, which is win.

I feel kind of privileged now, with my recently constructed Core i7 950 quad-core machine with HT overclocked to 3.8GHz, dual GTX460's in SLI (soon to be a 570), and 6GB of RAM...

EDIT: Oh! I got one more, a laptop thing. An HP Omnibook 800CT. It has almost identical specs to the 1996 machine above, just a 2GB hard disk instead (with some bad sectors). The lithium battery in it has long since gone flat. It had 95, but it also has DSL now.

Here's a pretty picture (not of mine, this one's a museum piece!):
[IMG]http://www.hpmuseum.net/images/OB800-33.jpg[/IMG]

---

### Post by ThatBum on 2011-01-14
> **Spice Weasel said:**
>  how big is it?

It's actually netbook sized, that keyboard has full sized keys. The dimensions closed are 28.2 cm x 18.5 cm x 4 cm (WDH). The screen is 10.4" 800x600. It weighs 1.8kg. When it was new, the battery lasted 3 hours. What's funny is it runs off straight 12VDC, unlike the higher voltages like 18VDC of today's laptops, so you can literally charge it off a car battery with just some wires. The funny thing about the display is that the pixels are really big, so things don't even look pixelated...they look...blockulated, like a mosaic.

But the very best bit is the mouse. It was before there was widespread touchpad technology. Do you see that black plastic thing sticking out by the Computer History sign? That's the mouse. It's incredibly brilliant how it works. See, there's nothing on the underside of the mouse but some Teflon feet. That black flat cable is actually a straight bar of rigid plastic with some PCB-type traces laminated to both sides. The top side has alternating slats of metal and insulator patterned horizontally on it. There is an inductive pickup sensor inside the laptop that detects how fast the slats of metal are going by when you move the mouse, therefore giving it a X axis. The Y axis is implemented with a linear potentiometer that is moved by the whole track for the rigid moving up and down (and not back and forth). The bottom trace has three wires, one for a left click, one for right, and a common ground. Another inductive pickup detects these to give you mouse clicks. The whole mouse can be collapsed and stored inside the laptop. The mouse and its bar are removable for replacement. The bar is not physically connected to anything due the the inductance, it just glides on some bearings. I suppose the disadvantage to this is that it needs a lot on internal space for the bar to move. But it works fantastically, and is very accurate. It feels nice in the hand, the buttons are crisp and tactile despite how old they are.

You might be wondering how I installed DSL on it with no media to install with. Well, it came with an external SCSI CD drive, and a proprietary HP connector external floppy drive. The BIOS doesn't boot from SCSI, so I had to do the poor man's install by booting the floppy image for DSL and manually moving all the install crap from the CD to the hard disk with the driver for the SCSI controller I had on the floppy. I then booted it from the hard disk, installed to a new partition, rebooted, wiped the partition I used for installation, and turned it into swap. Clever, eh?

---

### Post by Old_Grey_Wolf on 2011-02-23
> **robro said:**
> I have this:
[img]http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/TRS-80_Color_Computer_3.jpg/250px-TRS-80_Color_Computer_3.jpg[/img]
i dont even think this thing has ram :lolflag:

Ahhh, the TRS-80 Color Computer; also known as the CoCo.

I spent many happy hours programming on the older gray CoCo version 1 of the TRS-80 Color Computer. It had an 8-bit 6809 microprocessor and 16KB of RAM. The operating system was a BACIS program language interpreter store on 16KB of Read-Only-Memory (ROM).

My children played game on it.

Mine lasted for 9 years before the video converter died. It used a TV for a monitor.

At some point I bought a 80286 IBM clone.

---

### Post by ranch hand on 2011-04-01
> **Antarctica32 said:**
> Well now I have about 10 or so old Pentiums. A bunch of P3s and several really old laptops. IF you consider p4s old and crappy I have over 10 of them. All of these I got for my eagle project. 

[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1676724](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1676724)  

Here are some pics of the laptops. Most of them have no model numbers or names except for REC or DIGITAL in huge letters on the front. one has a pentium sticker. By far the best and most modern is an IBM Thinkpad t30 my dad brought home from work. I am currently attempting to put some kind of Linux on it. I tried 10.04 but it was incredibly laggy. Probably cuz it only has 256 RAM and 1.8GHz P4. I am trying to get some more ram. I will see how UNR is on it and if that doesn't work i will look into lubuntu or xubuntu if I have to. I really wish Lucid would work on it cuz im just so used to it and I really don't want to play around with unity. wish me luck.
I would not bother with Unity on anything that you have there.  Just not going to happen.  At this point it will not run on my box (specs in sig).  Can't even boot the image.

Lubuntu is trying to have something that will work on 256 and less ram.  It does require 256 to boot the install disk (I think even an alt disk).  They say it will run on lower ram than that but you have to do a netboot install (minimal install disk).  It is not a bad OS at all. 

You may want to try Debian Squeeze too.  It  is the new Debian Stable.  When 10.04 was developed it was based on Debian Testing (LTS releases based on testing - regular releases based on unstable) which at that time was Squeeze.  I think you will find it runs much better than 10.04.

The only thing that Ubuntu really has over Debian right now is a little better font rendering and an easier to understand sources.list.  Here is a link to a long standing thread that has folks sources list on it;
[http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/debian-26/post-your-source-list-330913/page14.html](http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/debian-26/post-your-source-list-330913/page14.html)
That link is to the last page so it is the area where you will find Squeeze, Wheezy (new testing 12.04 will be based on it) and of coarse probably sid and testing.

I run Debian testing.  It was Squeeze when I started.  if you replace Squeeze or Wheezy in the sources list with testing it will just be a rolling release of testing.  If I had left it as Squeeze I would now be running the current stable.  I like Debian testing very much better than 10.04.

### Post by ranch hand on 2011-04-04
UNR is now a thing of the past.  Unity which seems made for tablets is the default desktop on everything starting with 11.04.  There is the option on the login screen (bottom panel) to use Ubuntu Classic (Paneled Gnome Desktop).  That will be gone with 11.10.  You can install Unity2D if your box is not up to 3D (it is in the repo).

Unity is alright if you are not doing a lot on your box.  If you have mult-windows open that you have to work with regularly it is a pain.  Wubuntu or Lubuntu would be good.  On your box Lubuntu would probably run like a striped ape.  It will run on 256Mb ram.

### Post by eckeroo on 2011-04-16
Just got this up and running today:

motherboard:   A7N8X Deluxe rev2.0 BIOS 1008
memory:        2 x 512MB DDR PC2100 (soon to be 2 x 512MB DDR PC2700)
processor:     ATHLON XP 2600+ AXDA2600DKV3D
graphics card: INNO3D Geforce FX5500 256MB
hard disk:     Hitachi 160GB Cinemastar IDE HCS721616PLAT80

---some issues---

1) Trying to install Ubuntu through a usb boot was a real pain. Ubuntu 10.10 would hang after 'install' or 'live session' was selected. Instead, I used a usb boot with Ubuntu 10.04 which did work. With 10.04 installed I then upgraded to 10.10.

2) This motherboard doesn't take much provocation for it to reset the CMOS to default values.

3) Sometimes the usb ports are not activated after boot. Frustrating if you're trying to install Ubuntu using a boot USB memory stick.

4) Sometimes you get a blank screen for a DVI output. (using living room widescreen TV).

This motherboard required a lot more fiddling than the A7M266

---

### Post by ranch hand on 2011-04-16

processor:     ATHLON XP 2600+ AXDA2600DKV3D
graphics card: INNO3D Geforce FX5500 256MB
hard disk:     Hitachi 160GB Cinemastar IDE HCS721616PLAT80

---some issues---

1) Trying to install Ubuntu through a usb boot was a real pain. Ubuntu 10.10 would hang after 'install' or 'live session' was selected. Instead, I used a usb boot with Ubuntu 10.04 which did work. With 10.04 installed I then upgraded to 10.10.

2) This motherboard doesn't take much provocation for it to reset the CMOS to default values.

3) Sometimes the usb ports are not activated after boot. Frustrating if you're trying to install Ubuntu using a boot USB memory stick.

4) Sometimes you get a blank screen for a DVI output. (using living room widescreen TV).

This motherboard required a lot more fiddling than the A7M266
I had trouble with a bios that wanted to reset itself at one time too.  You may want ot look and see if you can set  password on your bios.  I know that stopped the resting for me.

Do not, as I did, forget that password.  That is a major pain.

### Post by Spice Weasel on 2011-04-21
> **honeybear said:**
> 
Would you know a X with less ram consumn. I would say about 60 MB ram in total... and a DX/SX

TinyCore runs X on less than 60. Not sure about DX/SX.

You will have to use GTK1 (not 2!), text user interface and FLTK applications if you don't want to go above 60.

Graphical Browser - Dillo (FLTK)
Graphical Editor - Editor (TinyCore built in. FLTK)
Text Music Player - mplayer (CLI), moc
Video Player - mplayer

---

### Post by ranch hand on 2011-04-21
> **phoenixcor said:**
> I pulled out a sony vaio thats about 10 years old.  windows on it is corrupt i think anyways.  i thought i would install ubuntu for my 8 year old daughter, but i cant get the system to boot from the cd and do a clean install.  

i have changed the bios priority, but it keeps trying to load windows which is not any good, and i cant get into the installation. steps.  

Any thoughts? I have used this particular iso image for many installs, so it should be a good clean image.  Thanks for any advice
Do you have an external drive and will your bios allow booting from a usb device.  If that is the case then install on it with another machine and hook it up, use gparted to format the internal and copy the partition(s) to the internal.  You will need to (probably) edit the fstab file to match the new environment.

Seems like the bios will probably not support usb booting if it does not support booting from a CD though.

### Post by Blonddeeni on 2011-07-01
For my 10 cents worth.
I have two old machines, a laptop given to me and a desktop that I literally found on the side of the road during a rubbish day.
(There were three, but used bits from one to fix two and three. #2 I gave away to the bro & co. They seem happy with it. It runs ubuntu? I can't remember).

The laptop has no cd or floppy drive. It has two card slots where apparently you can plug in an external cd-drive (now long lost) and modem. It has my $20 ethernet card in there at the moment.

1 usb-1 slot
CPU - Pentium III (Coppermine)
MHz 498
Ram 190M
HDD - 9G

Running PuppyLinux 5.0.1

It HAD windows XP on it. For a comparison, I timed it at 4 minutes 20sec to boot up.
Puppy does it in 1 minute 10 seconds.
I like puppy for the speed, but have major problems trying to install anything. (Like ssh).
To install an OS, I would remove the hard drive and put it into another dell laptop, do the install and then put the HD back in the old laptop : and hope.

The desktop I found on rubbish day:

CPU PIII Celeron (Coppermine)
MHz - 764
RAM - 120M
HDD - 7 G
Has a cd drive.

Had Windows 98 on it. Timed the boot-up but got bored after 5 minutes and wandered off.
Currently running Vector Linux. (1min 5 secs) A bit large for this machine. (Uses 3G of the 7G drive). Will probably do a "partimage save" and try something else.
The way I try is simply to see if an OS will install; then see how it goes.
Quite a few have trouble with the display, or memory or somesuch.
Like Damn Small Linux, worked great on the desktop, but refused to operate "MY.Dsl" and installing programs was a nightmare.

But that is the brilliance of the gnu/linux system. There are so many different OS's out there you can usually find something that will make your machine perform.
Featherlinux, dsl, puppy, slitaz, vector, various flavours of Ubuntu, and so on and so on.
For a noob like me I'm geeking out just thinking about it.:D
So many to try, so few to master.

Cheese Gromit! We'll go where there's CHEESE!

---

### Post by ninjaaron on 2011-09-02
Last year, I had to get a new laptop in a hurry (my old one stopped turning on on day), and I went in and bought the cheapest full-size laptop in the store.  However, it has been reliable and provided excellent performance for all of my needs, so I don't know if it counts for this thread. It has 3GB ram @800MHz, 2.2GHZ Pentium Dual Core 320GB SATA drive that seems plenty fast, a bunch of cheap intel cards that work perfectly with Linux, all the standard ports (w/ USB x3), multi-card reader, CD/DVD RW drive, full size keyboard with num pad.  The screen is pretty crappy, but it's big (15.6" wide-screen, 1366x768).  Speakers are loud.  The battery is already dying, but that's cause I end up leaving it plugged in most of the time.  Trackpad is single-touch, but it works perfectly well.  Everything is made of cheap plastic, but there hasn't been any malfunction.  The only think it doesn't do is play new video games that require a lot of acceleration, but that isn't really my thing (would like to try out SC2, however).

I don't know. It's the lowest of low-end, but it isn't crappy at all. It runs Linux the best of any computer I've tried.

### Post by LinuxFan999 on 2011-10-23
I have 4 old computers, and I will tell you about them.
 
Old Computer #1
 
Compaq Armada E500:
 
CPU: Pentium III @500 MHZ
RAM: 512 MB PC100
Graphics: ATI rage mobility with 8MB VRAM
HDD: 40GB IDE
Display: 14.1 inch 1024x768
Current OS: Haiku R1/alpha3
Previous OS's: Windows XP, Windows 98 SE, Puppy linux 4.1.2, Xubuntu 10.04, and Debian 6.0.1
 
This computer origionally came with Windows XP, but it was slow. later on, it lost audio output (it was a driver issue which i could not fix), and eventually got infected with a fake antivirus program (which seems to be the most common type of malware right now). I then removed XP and installed Windows 98 SE, which ran much faster than XP, but was buggy, and couldn't connect to the internet. I then tried puppy linux, but it refused to install, and had a cluttered desktop. Next, I tried Xubuntu 10.04, which ran fine fora while, then the system began to degrade, and eventually I wasn't able to log in. I then reinstalled Xubuntu, and it ran fine for a while, but eventually the bars at the top and bottom disappeared, and I couldn't bring them back, so I installed Debian with no GUI. Debian run well, but it had one problem, it refused to shut down. Typing the shutdown command ether did nothing, or after typing it, the system would tell me i was using it wrong (I actually had this same problem when testing a similar setup under Virtualbox), so there was no way to shut down the computer without pulling the power plug and battery. I then got tired of Debian and installed Haiku. Haiku runs smoothly on this computer, boots in 55 seconds and shuts down in 5 seconds. Everything appears to work correctly in Haiku. Haiku is the perfect Operating system for this computer.
 
Old Computer #2
 
HP pavilion 753n:
 
CPU: Pentium IV @2.53GHZ
RAM: 512MB PC2100 (DDR-266)
Graphics: Intel intergrated graphics.
HDD: 80GB IDE (noisy)
Display: HP f50
Current OS: Windows XP SP1
 
This computer was bought new in 2002 to replace a dying Gateway computer from the 90's. It has the highest specifications of the 4 computers on this list. It has 2 problems: 1. Windows XP is very sluggish on this machine (removing the bloatware didn't help). 2. Windows XP is also very unstable on this machine and randomly locks up during use. This computer has had these problems from the beginning. I have not used this computer for a long time. I have considered installing Ubuntu on this computer to help resolve the speed and stability problems, but I have never done it.
 
The next 2 Computers are macs which I got for free.
 
Old Computer #3
 
iBook G3 dual USB:
 
CPU: PowerPC G3 @500MHZ
RAM: 320MB PC100
Graphics: ATI rage mobility 8MB
HDD: 10GB IDE (noisy)
Display: 12inch 1024x768
Current OS's: Mac OS 9.22, Mac OSX 10.2.8, Windows 2000 running on Virtual PC 3
 
I got this computer from a school which was going to recycle it. It works perfectly, except I want to replace the hard drive, because it is too noisy.
 
Old Computer #4
 
Power Mac G3 Blue and white
 
CPU: PowerPC G3 @300MHZ
RAM: 512MB PC100 (origionally 384MB PC100)
Graphics: ATI rage 3D 16MB
HDD: 80GB IDE
Display: HP f50
Current OS: Mac OSX 10.2
 
I got this computer form the same school that I got the iBook forom. It was buried deep in a recycling pallate. When I turned it on, the origional 6GB hard drive spun up and clicked repetedly. The hard drive was replaced with an 80GB drive, and the memory was upgraded to 512MB. A Mac OSX 10.2 install CD was found in the disc tray. I then took the Computer home and installed Mac OSX on it. It works perfectly as well.

### Post by blueturtl on 2011-11-06
> **TeamRocket1233c said:**
> Hmm... Any kind of broadband would still be faster with better specs, and you can't do YouTube or TinierMe/GaiaOnline/etc. with ancient specs.

Actually the problem with YouTube is mostly Flash (it's too power hungry for the quality of the video transmitted). If you use either mplayerplug-in or the FlashVideoReplacer addon for Firefox you can play videos from YouTube in native MPlayer which will bring the hardware requirements down considerably. Most YouTube vids will play fine on 400 MHz and less even with MPlayer. :P

---

### Post by matt_symes on 2011-11-06
Hi

> If you use either mplayer-plugin

@blueturtl

Does mplayer already come with this plugin or do you need to install it separately ? Can you tell me what the plugin is called ? 

I have been after something like this.

Kind regards

---

### Post by blueturtl on 2011-11-06
@matt_symes

Last time I checked the easiest way to go was to just install MPlayer

```
sudo apt-get install mplayer
```

and then use the [FlashVideoReplacer addon]("https://addons.mozilla.org/en-US/firefox/addon/flashvideoreplacer/?src=ss") for Firefox. FlashVideoReplacer supports other video players besides MPlayer, but I've found MPlayer to be the fastest on really old computers. If MPlayer can't play it, your computer just doesn't have the muscle.

You can use the [mplayerplug-in]("http://mplayerplug-in.sourceforge.net/download.php") to cover sites which still use something other than Flash to play videos, but frankly it's a bit harder and also most of the time completely unnecessary.

FlashVideoReplacer should do the job just well.

If your computer is so dang old that the combination of web browser and video player bring it to it's knees, I would recommend a Firefox addon to just download the videos off YouTube and then playing the videos directly from your hard-drive MPlayer.

Hope this helps.

---

> OMG this is my next trial OS!! Effing amazing! I've got an ancient intel Pentium D "mini PC" that might need dusting off...

And to the Lubuntu lovers... you are about to rekindle my interest in Ubuntu. Please come up with a simple-ish "why" page near the top. Ubuntu > 10.04 LTS seems buggy every time I've tried it. Worse, the trial computers have always been for my lady or a new person at work... so when the OS locks up, it's pretty un-cool. Then throw in that Ubuntu ditched Gnome for Unity and I have dismissed Ubuntu for the future. Until 10.04 doesn't work anymore, I'm going to not care and then find a current distro that Just.Plain.Works.

Thanks!!
Chris
I would not recommend Ubuntu on an older box because of the use of Plymouth.   I would recommend Lxde (DE for Lubuntu) under Debian.

As far as something noob friendly for other folks try PCLOS.  It is from the RH branch of Linux but uses Apt for package management.

Ubuntu has not abandoned gnome for Unity.  Gnome3 is the base for Ubuntu.  The default Gnome DE for Gnome3 is Gnome Shell.  Ubuntu is using Unity.  Gnome Shell, like so many other things Ubuntu has quit installing, is available in the repo.

GS is somewhat better, I think, than Unity.  They both fail to blow my skirt up.   I use Xfce4 under Debian as my OS.

---

### Post by wb8nbs on 2011-12-30
I would like to report a successful install of 10.04 on a 1999 Thinkpad 770Z.  Install was from a magazine DVD.  I used the Netbook Remix option, it was the only one that would start from the DVD, probably due to limited memory.  At the time of install, the 770 had 320 megs of memory, I have since changed out the 64 meg DIMM on the motherboard for a 128 meg part so it now has 384 meg.  Browsing with Firefox 3.6 is slow but acceptable.

I also experimented with Puppy linux and Vector.  10.04 NBR seemed to work the best.  I did trial installs using a couple of spare hard drives. The final install was on a 40G fujitsu drive. I had a difficult time getting the 40G drive to boot, Grub2 did not like the partition alignment.

I had trouble getting the video to go to the native 1280x1024, copied in the xorg.conf from the previous Suse 10.3 install and video was then OK.

Tried 3 wireless G pcmcia cards, a Cisco which worked immediately, a Netgear and a COMPUSA card both of which worked after Ndiswrapper install using the Windows Wireless Drivers tool

Got sound to work using the directions at [http://www.thinkwiki.org/wiki/Installing_Ubuntu_7.04_Feisty_Fawn_on_a_ThinkPad_600E](http://www.thinkwiki.org/wiki/Installing_Ubuntu_7.04_Feisty_Fawn_on_a_ThinkPad_600E)  The NBR boot was loading an AC97 module and there ain't one in this machine.  The thinkwiki directions may work for other Thinkpads that have similar sound chips, 770X, 770Z, and most 600s.

I use this computer for web stuff.  I love the screen and keyboard.  Also installed the Arduino 1.0 IDE and will be using that in the future.

I moved the test hard drive (14G) to a Thinkpad 770E I own and looks like the NBR will work there also.  It has 100 megs less memory though, and may be too slow to use.

---

### Post by wb8nbs on 2012-01-04
> **wb8nbs said:**
> I would like to report a successful install of 10.04 on a 1999 Thinkpad 770Z.  Install was from a magazine DVD.  I used the Netbook Remix option, it was the only one that would start from the DVD, probably due to limited memory.  At the time of install, the 770 had 320 megs of memory, I have since changed out the 64 meg DIMM on the motherboard for a 128 meg part so it now has 384 meg.  Browsing with Firefox 3.6 is slow but acceptable.
.

Update: Installed the xubuntu-desktop this morning on the 770Z.  Top reported 336 megs in use on a cold boot with Gnome, down to 248 megs used with the XFCE desktop.  Firefox is significantly faster.  The install used an additional 300 megs or so of disk but installed a bunch of programs that Gnome didn't have (gimp, abiword).  It's a keeper.

### Post by amjjawad on 2012-01-12
> **wb8nbs said:**
> Update: Installed the xubuntu-desktop this morning on the 770Z.  Top reported 336 megs in use on a cold boot with Gnome, down to 248 megs used with the XFCE desktop.  Firefox is significantly faster.  The install used an additional 300 megs or so of disk but installed a bunch of programs that Gnome didn't have (gimp, abiword).  It's a keeper.

Installing the desktop package (xubuntu-desktop) is NOT like install the system (Xubuntu). You can try that yourself. Install Ubuntu then install the desktop package then install Xubuntu on new partitions and check the overall performance. 

I'm not saying "don't do that", I'm just sharing an observation which I'm not the only one who has noticed/observed that :)


By the way, I've done some test on P4 with 242MB RAM and SWAP OFF (so I force the system to use whatever RAM the PC has) and ... Ubuntu 10.04 was working BETTER than ... Xubuntu 11.04. I know what some of you may thing but I'm not a hater of other variant, I'm yet again sharing a real test I have done myself. 
Xubuntu is NOT the lightest. Lubuntu is light but still NOT the lightest. Lubuntu vs Xubuntu? no doubt Lubuntu is lighter than Xubuntu.

After all, if your system is capable of running both or even Unity then go for it but some people prefer simplicity over 3D stuff.

P.S.
Welcome to Ubuntu Forum :)

State of the Art - The hardware that you cannot afford
Obsolete - The hardware that you own
Microsecond - The time it takes for the former to become the latter :)

### Post by mrreality13 on 2012-03-31
any 1 see this yet?lol
[HTML]http://hackaday.com/2012/03/28/building-the-worst-linux-pc-ever/[/HTML]
Linux is generally considered the go-to OS for under powered computers. Wanting to challenge the preconceived notion that Linux requires ‘a computer made in the last 20 years,’ [Dmitry] built the worst Linux PC ever around a simple 8-bit microcontroller.

The ATMega1284p [Dmitry] used doesn’t have a lot to offer as far as RAM and storage goes; just 16 kilobytes of SRAM and a paltry 128 kilobytes of Flash storage. While this may be voluminous in the embedded world, it’s peanuts compared to the gigabytes of RAM and hard drive space on even a low-end netbook. To solve this problem, [Dmitry] threw an antique 30-pin RAM SIMM at the problem. It’s wired up directly to the microcontroller, as is the 1 Gigabyte SD card that serves as the PC’s hard drive.

Linux requires a 32-bit CPU and a memory management unit, something the puny microcontroller doesn’t have. For [Dmitry], the best course of action was emulating an ARM processor on an AVR. We’re not sure if we’re dealing with genius or madness here, but it did prove to be a valuable learning exercise in writing a modular ARM emulator.

How fast is it? [Dmitry] tells us**[COLOR="DarkSlateBlue"] it takes two hours to boot up to a bash prompt, and four more to load up Ubuntu and login.[/COLOR]** If you want a Megahertz rating, good luck; the effective clock speed is about 6.5 kilohertz. While the worst Linux PC ever won’t win any races, its simple construction puts it within the reach of even the klutziest of hardware builders; the entire device is just a microcontroller, RAM, SD card, a few resistors, and some wire.

---

### Post by Fraoch on 2012-05-06
Seeing that this old thread isn't quite dead...

I got a new processor and motherboard recently and have been moving the old hardware down to secondary uses.  The oldest hardware I'm working on right now is:

[ECS K7SOM+]("http://www.ecs.com.tw/ECSWebSite/Product/Product_Detail.aspx?DetailID=242&CategoryID=1&MenuID=24&LanID=0")

It's got a 1.2 GHz AMD Duron soldered into it.  I got it because I was looking for a secondary computer and didn't want to spend much - it was only $60 at the time and came with a CPU soldered in.  I currently have maxed it out with 1 GB of DDR RAM.

It's borderline whether it will run Ubuntu so I tried Xubuntu.  Problem is the installer fails at the same spot every time, I think an application being installed doesn't like the hardware.  Repeatable on both USB and DVD, repeatable even with the alternate installer.  I tried Lubuntu, same problem.  I was about to try Linux Mint but I notice it uses the same installer so I didn't waste my time.

I then tried Debian and it works!  It's kind of slow but it does seem to be functional.  I'm using it to write this post.

I have *even older* hardware but no case for it now - an AMD K6-II 400 MHz with 192 MB of RAM.  I did have an old version of Xubuntu running (very slowly) on it.  Most likely that wouldn't work now.  I would try Lubuntu maybe, but that might be a stretch.  Puppy Linux?  That works fairly well on my wife's old 366 MHz Celeron laptop.

I did an experiment about a year ago using a VM to see which distro combination would run nicely on that 192 MB of RAM.  I built several distros from base installations and monitored memory usage.  The winner was Gentoo with Openbox and fbpanel...only 35 MB of RAM!  A close second was Arch Linux with Openbox and fbpanel.  It might be nice to try Gentoo on this hardware (the Duron) but it would take ages to compile everything.  An Intel Core 2 Duo E6600 with Gentoo in a VM took days to do it.  The Duron might take weeks...the K6-II I would have no idea.  My only hope would be to use distcc to move heavy compilation to more powerful machines on the network.

I like old computers.  They can still be pretty useful.  My *oldest* hardware is a Pentium 100!  I think it's got 60 MB of EDO RAM.  I ran a ClarkConnect firewall on it for a while, then SmoothWall.  Looks like SmoothWall might still run on it, or perhaps m0n0wall.  It's a noisy Baby AT case though, I couldn't stand it after a while.

---

### Post by Borunco on 2012-10-28
Hi cajunlibra, I'm not sure if I missed it in your post, but did you make sure that you have your CD/DVD player to boot #1 in your bios and if you have a usb hdd option make that #2 and #3 your regular Hdd. If that's the way you have it. It should boot the live cd. If you try to boot the usb without that (#2 option) it will not boot. You can take a look at this tutorial [Boot From a USB Drive Even if your BIOS Won’t Let You - How-To Geek. ]("http://www.howtogeek.com/howto/16822/boot-from-a-usb-drive-even-if-your-bios-wont-let-you/")
See if that will help. most of the older boards will not let you boot from usb, just because you have usb ports don't mean you can boot of them. Any ways if you try to install Ubuntu make sure you have enough ram on your system or it will not install. you can always try Lubuntu, that will make your old box screem. :)
I hope this helps
borunco

---

### Post by Borunco on 2012-10-29
Hi cajunlibra, I would suggest that you might open a new thread, so it could be seen by more people. This thread here does not get much action. as a last quick check. I know that you said that the live cd was find. OK, but do you have any other live cd, that you can try to see if you get the same results?. the reason why I'm saying this is because that has happen to me and just because the one you have works on your laptop doesn't necessarily mean that it will work on your PC just because of the difference in hard ware. and did you make sure when the cd was made that it was written at x4 speed, that also has a lot to do with it. if none of what I suggested works please open a new thread so you can get more people to look at it.
Thank you
borunco
